The Sixth Purpose: Sam'iyyat and Its Four Chapters
Taftazani introduces the sixth purpose and lists its four sections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scope of the sam'iyyat (revelational matters) in Islamic theology and the four main topics it covers according to Taftazani.

Core Idea
Revelational matters are the core of Islamic theology — truths beyond reason.

In this lesson, we begin exploring the sixth and final purpose of Taftazani's Maqasid — the sam'iyyat, or matters known through revelation. This passage introduces the overall structure, listing the four major investigations that follow. Understanding this map will help you navigate the detailed discussions of prophethood, resurrection, divine names and rulings, and leadership.

Guided Path
1
Read and familiarize
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of Taftazani's concise style. Notice how he introduces the topic.
2
Identify the four investigations
List the four investigations mentioned. Look for the words 'أربعة مباحث' which signal the enumeration.
The Arabic word 'مباحث' means investigations or topics.
3
Reflect on the scope
Consider why these four topics are grouped under 'sam'iyyat'. What do they have in common?
Sam'iyyat are matters known through revelation, so all four are based on transmitted sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السمعيات ) قال : المقصد السادس) في السمعيات وفيه فصول. أربعة مباحث : النبوة، ومباحث المعاد، ومباحث الأسماء والأحكام وما يلائمها، ومباحث الإمامة.
The sam'iyyat. He said: The sixth purpose: on the sam'iyyat, and in it are chapters. Four investigations: prophethood, investigations of resurrection, investigations of names and rulings and what is suitable to them, and investigations of imamate.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage classifies the contents of the sixth purpose (al-maqsad al-sadis) of the book, which deals with sam'iyyat (revelational matters). It states that the sixth purpose consists of chapters (fusul), and then enumerates four investigations (mabahith): first, prophethood (nubuwwa); second, matters of resurrection (ma'ad); third, matters of names (asma') and rulings (ahkam) and what is suitable to them, which likely includes issues of faith and sin; fourth, matters of imamate (imama). This enumeration provides the overall structure of the section on sam'iyyat.

Key Terms
السمعيات
المقصد السادس
النبوة
المعاد
الأسماء والأحكام
الإمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the four investigations covered in the sixth purpose according to this passage?
A) Prophethood, resurrection, names and rulings, imamate
B) Prophethood, miracles, eschatology, imamate
C) Divine attributes, prophethood, resurrection, imamate
D) Resurrection, names, rulings, angelology

Key Takeaway
The sixth purpose of the Maqasid is devoted to sam'iyyat and covers four investigations: prophethood, resurrection, names and rulings, and imamate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the four investigations in the sam'iyyat do you most struggle to fully accept or understand, and why? What one step will you take this week to strengthen your conviction in it?
?
When you think about the Day of Resurrection (ma'ad), does it fill you with hope or fear? How does that feeling change how you live today—and is there a habit you need to change because of it?
?
The investigation of imamate touches on leadership in the Muslim community. Are you more concerned with who leads, or with how you follow? What would it look like for you to be a more responsible follower of true Islamic leadership?

Refuting the Objection of Ambiguous Verses
The author cites and refutes the objection that the Quran contains ambiguous verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars respond to the objection that ambiguous verses in the Quran are problematic, and see their positive role.

Core Idea
Ambiguous verses invite deeper reflection, not confusion.

This short passage from the author addresses a common objection raised against the Quran: the presence of ambiguous verses (mutashabihat). Critics argue that these verses allow misinterpretation by deviant groups. The author refutes this, showing that these verses serve a beneficial purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the objection and response.
The Arabic is short but dense; take time with each phrase.
2
Identify the objection
Note what the objector claims: ambiguous verses lead misguidant groups like anthropomorphists to misinterpret.
The verse cited is about Allah's istiwa' (establishing Himself) over the Throne.
3
Understand the refutation
See how the author responds: these verses reward reflection and require returning to scholars (al-rasikhun fi al-'ilm).
The refutation shows the verses have a purpose, not a flaw.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why this defense is important for Muslim theology and how it encourages seeking knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن فيه متشابهات يتمسك بها أهل الغواية كالمجسمة بمثل : الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى (۳). ورد بأنها لنيل المثوبة بالنظر والاجتهاد في طلب المراد، أو لفوائد لا تحصى بالرجوع إلى الراسخين في العلم .
And among them is that it (the Quran) contains ambiguous verses (mutashabihat) which the people of misguidance, like the anthropomorphists (al-mujassimah), cling to, such as: 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne' (20:5). And it was responded that they are for attaining reward through reflection and exertion in seeking the intended meaning, or for countless benefits by referring to those firmly grounded in knowledge (al-rasikhun fi al-'ilm).
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the Quran and its refutation. The objector claims that the Quran contains ambiguous verses (mutashabihat) that deviant groups like the anthropomorphists (al-mujassimah) use to support their erroneous beliefs, citing the verse about Allah's istiwa' (establishing Himself) over the Throne. The author responds that these ambiguous verses are not a flaw but a positive feature. They serve to encourage believers to exert effort (ijtihad) in seeking the correct interpretation, thereby earning reward. Alternatively, they provide benefits that are countless when one refers to those firmly grounded in knowledge (al-rasikhun fi al-'ilm), that is, the scholars who can correctly interpret them. Thus, the presence of mutashabihat does not detract from the Quran but rather enhances its purpose of guiding people through proper understanding.

Key Terms
متشابهات
المجسمة
الراسخون في العلم
الاستواء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think ambiguous verses in the Quran are errors or meaningless, allowing any interpretation.
Correction: They are purposeful: they reward reflection and require referring to scholars, not arbitrary misinterpretation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the purpose of ambiguous verses (mutashabihat) in the Quran?
A) They are errors that need to be corrected.
B) They allow deviant groups like the anthropomorphists to misinterpret.
C) They are for attaining reward through reflection and ijtihad, or for benefits by referring to scholars.
D) They are meant to be ignored.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's ambiguous verses serve to reward reflection and direct believers to scholars, not to cause misguidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you come across a verse in the Quran that you do not immediately understand, do you feel frustration or humble curiosity? How does your reaction align with the purpose of ambiguous verses described in this lesson? Commit to one specific action you will take next time to refer to those firmly grounded in knowledge.
?
Reflect on a time you relied on your own understanding of a difficult verse rather than seeking a qualified scholar. What was the outcome? How might it have been different if you had followed the path of the ‘rasikhun’? What is one step you can take this week to strengthen your connection to reliable Islamic scholarship?
?
The anthropomorphists cling to verses like 'Istiwa' to support their errors. In what ways might you be clinging to comfortable interpretations of Allah’s attributes that suit your own human experience? Are you willing to accept that some aspects of Allah are beyond human comprehension? Pray for guidance to embrace the correct understanding.

Repetition in the Quran: Flaw or Beauty?
The author presents and refutes the objection of repetition in the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why repetition in the Quran is not a flaw but a deliberate rhetorical device according to scholars of rhetoric.

Core Idea
Repetition in the Quran is a mark of eloquence, not a defect.

Among the objections raised against the Quran is the claim that it contains useless repetition. Critics point to repeated stories and verses as evidence. However, scholars of rhetoric consider such repetition a beauty of speech. This lesson examines the objection and the reasoned response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the objector's claim that the Quran contains the flaw of repetition. Notice the examples given: the story of Pharaoh and repeated verses.
The objector uses specific instances to argue that repetition diminishes the Quran's quality.
2
Identify the examples
Focus on the two examples: the repeated story of Pharaoh in multiple places, and the repeated verses in Surat al-Rahman and al-Mursalat. Understand why the objector sees this as a flaw.
Repetition might seem redundant to some, but it serves a purpose.
3
Understand the refutation
The author responds by stating that such repetition is among the beauties of speech (mahasin al-kalam) according to scholars of rhetoric (al-bayan). Reflect on how repetition can be a rhetorical tool.
Rhetoric values emphasis, reinforcement, and audience impact.
4
Connect to rhetoric
Consider the broader principle: repetition in the Quran is not accidental but intentional, serving to reinforce key themes and facilitate reflection.
This lesson shows that apparent flaws may have deeper wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن فيه عيب التكرار كإعادة قصة فرعون في عدة مواضع، وكإعادة : فَبِأَيِّ آلَاءِ رَبِّكُمَا تُكَذِّبَانِ (٤) ووَيْلٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ لِلْمُكَذِّبِينَ () في سورة الرحمن والمرسلات. ورد بأنه ربما يكون من محاسن الكلام على ما يقرره علماء البيان فيما وقع منه في القرآن .
And among them is that it contains the flaw of repetition, such as recounting the story of Pharaoh in several places, and such as repeating 'So which of the favors of your Lord would you deny?' (55:16) and 'Woe that Day to the deniers' (77:15) in Surat al-Rahman and Surat al-Mursalat. And it was responded that perhaps it is among the beauties of speech, according to what the scholars of rhetoric (al-bayan) have established concerning that which occurs in the Quran.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage presents another objection to the Quran: that it contains the flaw of repetition. The objector gives examples: the repeated story of Pharaoh in multiple surahs, and the repeated verses 'So which of the favors of your Lord would you deny?' in Surat al-Rahman and 'Woe that Day to the deniers' in Surat al-Mursalat. The author refutes this by stating that such repetition is actually among the beauties of speech, as established by scholars of rhetoric (al-bayan). Repetition in the Quran is not a flaw but a rhetorical device that emphasizes important themes and serves an eloquence purpose.

Key Terms
التكرار
علماء البيان
محاسن الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Repetition in the Quran is a flaw that indicates lack of eloquence or redundancy.
Correction: Repetition in the Quran is a deliberate rhetorical device that reinforces key messages and is considered a beauty of speech by scholars of rhetoric.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that the Quran contains repetition?
A) He admits it is a flaw but says it is unavoidable.
B) He denies that there is any repetition.
C) He argues that repetition is a beauty of speech according to rhetorical scholars.
D) He says it is only apparent to non-believers.

Key Takeaway
Repetition in the Quran is not a defect but a rhetorical beauty that reinforces key messages.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that the Quran repeats itself unnecessarily? How does this lesson change your view on those passages? Which verse from Surah Ar-Rahman or Al-Mursalat have you read without feeling its weight, and how will you recite it differently today?
?
When you hear or recite 'So which of the favors of your Lord would you deny?' repeatedly, do you truly let it question your gratitude? What blessing have you taken for granted that this repetition should bring to your attention right now?
?
The scholars of rhetoric see beauty in repetition. But in your daily life, do you find the repeated calls to prayer, the daily recitations, or even the recurring trials boring? How will you train your heart to see the wisdom and beauty in what Allah repeats to you?

Refuting the Objection of Discrepancy in the Quran
The author responds to an objection about Quranic variant readings by defining 'ikhtilaf' and analyzing the logic of the verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of 'ikhtilaf' in Quran 4:82 and how the logical analysis of 'law' refutes the objection based on variant readings.

Core Idea
The Quran’s denial of discrepancy is about eloquence, not recitation variants.

The objector points to thousands of variant readings in the Quran, arguing they contradict the verse that denies discrepancy. This lesson unpacks the precise meaning of 'ikhtilaf' in the verse and the logical structure of the argument. You will see how the author uses the conditional 'law' to preserve the proof while addressing the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
First, read the Arabic passage and translation to get the full argument. Notice the objector's claim about variant readings exceeding twelve thousand.
The variant readings refer to qira'at.
2
Understand the definition of 'ikhtilaf'
The author explains that the discrepancy denied in the verse is disparity in levels of eloquence (tafawut fi maratib al-balaghah), not differences in recitation. This is the key to refuting the objection.
3
Analyze the logical role of 'law'
The author clarifies two possible interpretations of 'law' in the verse: one negates the consequence due to the negated condition (default reading), the other infers from the negation of the necessary condition to the negation of the conditioned (as in 21:22). Understand both to see how the proof remains valid.
4
Reflect on the dialectical exchange
Notice how the author counters the objection that the argument is invalid by showing it is based on linguistic usage or logical inference. The passage ends with a reference to a fuller treatment in Talkhis al-Miftah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن فيه قوله : وَلَوْ كَانْ مِنْ عِنْدِ غَيْرِ اللَّهِ لَوَجَدُوا فِيهِ اخْتِلافَاً كثيراً﴾ (٦) وأنت تجد فيه من الاختلاف المسموع من أصحاب القراءة ما يربى على اثني عشر ألفاً . ورد بأن المراد من الاختلاف المنفي هو التفاوت في مراتب البلاغة، بحيث يكون بعضة قاصراً عن مرتبة الإعجاز ، لا يقال : تقدير الطعن فاسد عن أصله، لأنه استدلال بثبوت اللازم على ثبوت الملزوم لأنا نقول: لا، بل هو مبني على أن كلمة « لو» في اللغة تفيد انتفاء الجزاء لانتفاء الشرط، يعني عدم وجدان الاختلاف فيه بسبب أنه ليس من عند غير الله . وأما إذا حملت كلمة «لو» في الآية على ما هو قانون الاستدلال كما في قوله تعالى : لَوْ كَانَ فِيهِمَا آلِهَةً إِلَّا اللهُ لَفَسَدَتَا (١). فهو استدلال بنفي اللازم على نفي الملزوم، أي لكن لم يوجد فيه الاختلاف، فلم يكن من عند غير الله، وتمام تحقيق هذا المقام يطلب من شرحنا لتلخيص المفتاح،
And among them is that it (the Quran) contains His saying: 'If it had been from other than Allah, they would have found therein much discrepancy' (4:82), while you find in it from the discrepancy heard from the reciters what exceeds twelve thousand. And it was responded that what is meant by the denied discrepancy is the disparity in levels of eloquence, such that some of it falls short of the level of inimitability. It cannot be said: the estimation of the objection is invalid from its root because it is an inference from the existence of the necessary condition to the existence of the conditioned, for we say: No, rather it is based on the fact that the word 'law' (if) in the language indicates the negation of the consequence due to the negation of the condition, meaning the non-existence of discrepancy in it because it is not from other than Allah. But if the word 'law' in the verse is interpreted according to the principle of inference, as in His saying: 'If there had been within them gods besides Allah, they would have been ruined' (21:22), then it is an inference from the negation of the necessary condition to the negation of the conditioned, i.e., but discrepancy has not been found in it, so it was not from other than Allah. And the complete investigation of this topic is sought from our commentary on Talkhis al-Miftah.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage presents a more complex objection and response. The objector cites the Quranic verse that denies discrepancy (ikhtilaf) in the Quran, yet notes that there are many variant readings (qira'at) among reciters, which appear to be discrepancies. The author responds that the term 'ikhtilaf' in the verse refers specifically to disparity in eloquence (tafawut fi maratib al-balaghah), meaning that if the Quran were from other than Allah, some parts would be less eloquent than the level of i'jaz (inimitability). The variant readings do not involve differences in eloquence. The author then addresses a potential objection: that the objector's argument is invalid because it infers the existence of the condition (the Quran being from other than Allah) from the existence of the necessary condition (discrepancy). But the author argues that the word 'law' in the verse is used to negate the consequence due to negation of condition: i.e., the absence of discrepancy proves that it is not from other than Allah. Alternatively, if 'law' is interpreted as in the verse about gods (21:22), it becomes an inference from the negation of the necessary condition to the negation of the conditioned: since no discrepancy is found, it is not from other than Allah. The author concludes by referring readers to his own commentary on Talkhis al-Miftah for a fuller treatment.

Key Terms
الاختلاف
البلاغة
الإعجاز
لو
الاستدلال
اللازم
الملزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The variant readings (qira'at) are a form of discrepancy (ikhtilaf) that the Quran itself denies.
Correction: The verse denies disparity in eloquence (tafawut fi maratib al-balaghah), not differences in recitation. Variant readings are not a contradiction to the verse.

Checkpoint
What does the author say is meant by 'ikhtilaf' (discrepancy) in Quran 4:82?
A) Differences in qira'at (recitations)
B) Disparity in levels of eloquence
C) Contradictions in rulings
D) Variations in stories

Key Takeaway
The Quran's denial of discrepancy is about eloquence, and the conditional 'law' can be interpreted either as negating consequence due to negated condition or as negating the conditioned from negated necessary condition, both preserving the proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the variant readings of the Quran, do you ever feel a twinge of doubt about its preservation? How does this lesson's distinction between recitation variants and eloquence discrepancy address that feeling? Will you now view the qira'at as a sign of richness rather than a problem?
?
This passage presents a logical defense of the Quran divine origin. Have you ever faced an objection to your faith that seemed convincing at first? How can you use the logical principles shown here (defining terms correctly, analyzing conditional statements) to respond to such objections? Commit to learning one more logical tool from the Islamic tradition to strengthen your conviction.

Refuting Claims of Contradiction in the Quran
The author lists examples of alleged contradiction and gives the refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Muslim theologians refute apparent contradictions in the Quran by denying that the strict conditions for logical contradiction are met.

Core Idea
Apparent Quranic contradictions fail because the conditions for contradiction are not present.

Critics of the Quran often point to verses that seem to conflict. The author acknowledges these examples but counters by invoking the logical principle that contradiction requires specific conditions—conditions that are not actually present in these cases. This lesson examines the objection and the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two pairs of verses used as examples.
2
Identify the alleged contradictions
The passage gives two examples: one about questioning on Judgment Day, and one about food in Hell. Notice how each pair seems to conflict.
3
Understand the refutation
The author responds by denying that the conditions for contradiction (shurūṭ al-tanāquḍ) are present. This is a logical argument: contradiction requires exact opposition in the same respect, which these verses do not satisfy.
The author refers to tafsir books for detailed explanations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن فيه التناقض كقوله تعالى : فيَوْمَئِدٍ لا يُسْأَلُ عَنْ ذَنْبِهِ إِنْسٌ وَلَا جَانٌ) (۲) مع قوله: ﴿فَوَرَبِّكَ لَنَسْأَلَنَّهُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ عَمَّا كَانُوا يَعْمَلُونَ ) (۳) لَيْسَ لَهُمْ طَعَامُ إِلا مِنْ ضَريع (4) مع قوله : وَلَا طَعَامُ إِلا مِنْ غِسْلِين (٥) . إلى غير ذلك من مواضع فيها تنافي الكلامين . ورد بمنع وجود شرائط التناقض . وقد بين ذلك على التفصيل في كتب التفسير.
And among them is that it contains contradiction, such as His saying: 'Then on that Day, no soul will be asked about its sin, neither human nor jinn' (55:39) along with His saying: 'But by your Lord, We will surely question them all about what they used to do' (15:92-93), and 'They will have no food except from thorny plants (dari')' (88:6) along with His saying: 'And no food except from pus (ghislin)' (69:36). And so on from other places where there is conflict between the two statements. And it was responded by denying the existence of the conditions for contradiction. And that has been explained in detail in the books of tafsir.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage presents the objection that the Quran contains contradictions (tanāquḍ). Examples are given: verse 55:39 says no one will be asked about sin, while 15:92-93 says all will be questioned. Also, 88:6 says food is dari' (thorny plants), while 69:36 says food is ghislin (pus). The author responds by denying that the conditions for actual logical contradiction are present. In logic, contradiction requires that two propositions be exactly opposite in the same respect. These apparent conflicts can be reconciled through contextual and exegetical considerations. The author refers to detailed explanations in tafsir books for full resolution.

Key Terms
التناقض
شرائط التناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some critics claim that the Quran contains genuine logical contradictions that cannot be resolved.
Correction: The Quran does not contain contradictions because the strict logical conditions for contradiction are not met; apparent conflicts are resolved through proper interpretation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the author respond to claims of contradiction in the Quran?
A) By accepting the contradictions and explaining them away.
B) By denying that the conditions for contradiction (shurūṭ al-tanāquḍ) are met.
C) By rewriting the verses to remove conflict.
D) By saying the verses are abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The refutation of alleged Quranic contradictions relies on the logical principle that contradiction has specific conditions, which are not fulfilled in these examples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever encountered an apparent contradiction in the Quran that made you doubt its truthfulness, even for a moment? How does today's lesson challenge you to respond with trust in Allah's wisdom rather than skepticism?
?
When you read verses that seem conflicting, do you instinctively assume a problem exists, or do you actively seek the condition (zaman, makan, etc.) that resolves them? What does your reaction reveal about your relationship with the Quran?
?
If someone came to you tomorrow citing one of these examples to attack the Quran, would you be able to defend it with the logical principle of شرائط التناقض? If not, what step will you take this week to internalize this refutation?

Refuting the Objection of Falsehood in the Quran
Taftazani refutes the claim that Quran 7:11 contains falsehood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars refute the objection that a Quranic verse contains falsehood by properly identifying the referent of the pronoun.

Core Idea
Apparent falsehood in the Quran is resolved by correct grammatical reference.

Objectors sometimes claim that the Quran contains a false statement in verse 7:11, arguing that the timing of the command to prostrate does not match the creation of the addressees. In this passage, the author provides a clear refutation by explaining the grammatical referent of the pronoun. This shows how careful attention to context resolves apparent contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the structure of the objection and response become clear.
2
Understand the objection
The objector claims that the verse implies the command to prostrate occurred after the creation of the addressees, which contradicts known history. Identify what the objector thinks is false.
3
Understand the response
The author responds that the pronoun 'you' refers to Adam, not to the current audience. This removes the apparent contradiction. Notice how a single grammatical clarification resolves the issue.
This refutation does not require abrogation or metaphorical interpretation.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider how this shows that apparent issues in the Quran often arise from misunderstanding language or context, not from actual error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن فيه الكذب المحض كقوله تعالى : وَلَقَدْ خَلَقْنَاكُمْ ثُمَّ صَوَّرْنَاكُمْ ثُمَّ قُلْنَا لِلْمَلَائِكَةِ اسْجُدُوا لِآدَمَ ) (٦) للقطع بأن الأمر بالسجود لم يكن بعد خلقنا وتصويرنا . ورد بأن المراد خلق أبينا آدم وتصويره .
And among them is that it contains pure falsehood, such as His saying: 'And We have certainly created you, then formed you, then said to the angels, "Prostrate to Adam"' (7:11) because it is certain that the command to prostrate was not after our creation and formation. And it was responded that what is meant is the creation and formation of our father Adam.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection that the Quran contains a false statement (kadhib mahd). The objector points to verse 7:11, which says 'We created you, then formed you, then said to the angels: Prostrate to Adam.' The objector argues that this is false because the command to prostrate to Adam occurred before the creation of the addressees (the believers). The author responds that the verse is not false because the 'you' (kum) refers not to the current audience but to Adam, our father. The creation and formation mentioned are Adam's creation and formation, and the command to the angels to prostrate followed that. Thus, there is no falsehood.

Key Terms
الكذب المحض
خلق آدم
السجود لآدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 7:11 addresses all humans individually, implying an error in the sequence of events.
Correction: The verse actually refers to Adam, the father of humanity, so the command to prostrate follows his creation logically.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct interpretation of 'We created you, then formed you, then said to the angels: Prostrate to Adam' (7:11)?
A) It refers to the creation of all humans individually.
B) It contains an error about the order of events.
C) 'You' refers to Adam, our father.
D) It is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The apparent falsehood in Quran 7:11 is resolved by correctly identifying the pronoun 'you' as referring to Adam, not to the current audience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a Quranic verse that seems difficult or contradictory at first glance, do you rush to doubt or do you pause and seek a proper interpretation? How can you train yourself to trust Allah's words and investigate deeper before forming an opinion?
?
The objector in this passage assumed falsehood based on a superficial reading. Are there areas in your life—whether in religious matters or daily interactions—where you make quick judgments without seeking deeper understanding? How can you commit to being more thorough?
?
The correct understanding of this verse hinges on a simple grammatical point. What does this teach you about the precision and depth of the Quran? Will you make a habit of studying tafsir and Arabic to avoid misunderstandings that could weaken your faith?

Abrogation for a Renewed Benefit
Response that abrogation is for a newly arising benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why abrogation is permissible as a response to changed human circumstances, not a change in divine knowledge.

Core Idea
Abrogation reflects changed human interests, not a change in God's mind.

The objection that abrogation implies badā' (change in God's knowledge) is a serious theological challenge. Sanusi answers by explaining that abrogation occurs for a benefit that newly arises with changing times and conditions. This passage presents that answer with a vivid medical analogy and a scriptural example from the Torah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the structure: an answer, a general principle, an analogy, and an example.
Notice the concise style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage begins with 'And the answer: that it is for a benefit that renewed...' This is the core response to the objection. Identify what benefit (maslahah) means here.
Benefit here refers to the welfare of creation, not a change in God.
3
Understand the reasoning
Sanusi explains that interests (masālih) differ according to times and conditions. He uses a medical analogy: some medicine suits summer but not winter, and suits Zayd but not 'Amr. Reflect on how this supports the idea of abrogation.
The analogy shows that changing rulings is rational and reflects wisdom.
4
Consider the Torah example
The passage cites an example from the Torah: Adam was commanded to marry his daughters to his sons, but this was later abrogated by consensus. This illustrates that abrogation occurred even in earlier scriptures.
This example grounds the argument in revealed history.
5
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider why this answer resolves the objection of badā'. If God's commands change based on changing interests, His knowledge remains perfect and unchanged—He always knew when each command was appropriate.
Abrogation highlights God's wisdom, not ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أنه لمصلحة تجددت وحصلت بعد ما لم تكن، فإن المصالح تختلف باختلاف الأزمان والأحوال . فرب دواء يصلح في الصيف دون الشتاء، ولزيد دون عمرو، ولهذا أورد في التوراة أن آدم أمر بتزويج بناته من بنيه ، ثم نسخ وفاقاً .
And the answer: that it is for a benefit that renewed and occurred after it had not been, for interests differ according to differences in times and conditions. For some medicine is suitable in summer but not winter, and for Zayd but not 'Amr, and for this it was mentioned in the Torah that Adam was commanded to marry his daughters to his sons, then it was abrogated by agreement.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection against the permissibility of abrogation. The objection claimed that abrogation involves changing God's command, implying badā' (change in divine knowledge). The answer explains that abrogation occurs for a benefit (maslahah) that newly arises and did not exist before. Benefits vary according to time and circumstance—just as a medicine may be suitable in summer but not winter, and for one person but not another. An example is given from the Torah: Adam was commanded to marry his daughters to his sons, but this was later abrogated by consensus. This shows that abrogation does not imply change in God's knowledge but reflects the changing nature of created interests.

Key Terms
مصلحة
نسخ
توقيت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abrogation implies that God changed His mind or that His knowledge is imperfect.
Correction: Abrogation occurs for a renewed benefit (maslahah) that did not exist before. God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging; He always knew which command would be appropriate for each time and circumstance.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the reason for abrogation?
A) God changed His mind
B) For a renewed benefit that did not exist before
C) Because earlier prophets made mistakes
D) To test the believers

Key Takeaway
Abrogation occurs for a benefit that newly arises according to varying times and conditions, not because of a change in divine knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a religious ruling that has changed over time (e.g., different scholarly opinions), do you feel unsettled because it seems like God changed His mind? How can you train yourself to trust that His wisdom adapts to changing human needs without compromising His perfection?
?
The passage uses a medical analogy: what is beneficial in summer may harm in winter. In your own spiritual journey, are there practices that once helped you grow but now feel stale? Are you willing to let go of them and seek what benefits your current condition, even if it feels unfamiliar?
?
Think of a religious stance you hold strongly today. If future circumstances made that stance harmful to your faith or community, would you be open to reconsidering it based on a deeper understanding of God's purpose? Or would you cling to it out of habit, forgetting that God's commands are for human benefit, not His own?

Definition and Description of Angels
Defines angels and lists their characteristics and points of disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic definition of angels, their key characteristics, and the points of disagreement regarding their infallibility and superiority over prophets.

Core Idea
Angels are honored, obedient, genderless bodies capable of superhuman acts.

This passage from Sanusi's creed presents a concise definition of angels. It outlines their essential attributes and notes two areas of theological debate. As you read, focus on how the definition affirms their physical nature while denying them gender, a key point distinguishing Islamic angelology from other traditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's concise style register.
2
Identify each characteristic
List the characteristics of angels as mentioned: honored servants, persistent obedience, various forms, ability for difficult actions, living bodies, and no gender.
Notice that 'no gender' is a distinctive claim.
3
Note the two disagreements
The passage mentions two points of debate: whether angels are infallible (ma'sum) and whether they are superior to prophets (afdal min al-anbiya').
These are topics of theological discussion within the community.

Arguments for Angelic Infallibility and Their Refutation
Presents proof texts for infallibility and three objections with answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how proponents and opponents of angelic infallibility use scriptural evidence and how classical scholars reconcile apparent contradictions.

Core Idea
Objections to angelic infallibility are answered by careful analysis of scriptural examples.

This lesson presents a classic debate in Islamic theology: Are angels infallible? Proponents cite clear Quranic verses, while opponents raise three objections: the case of Iblis, the angels' question about the caliph, and the story of Harut and Marut. The answers show how each objection is resolved through scriptural interpretation and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof texts and objections
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first a proof for infallibility, then three objections, then three answers.
The passage is dense, so read slowly.
2
Understand the first objection (Iblis) and its answer
The first objection claims that Iblis was an angel who disobeyed. The answer explains that Iblis is from the jinn, not an angel by nature, and was only counted among them by predominance.
This shows the principle of taghlib in scriptural interpretation.
3
Understand the second objection (angels' question) and its answer
The second objection claims the angels' question was backbiting and self-admiration. The answer clarifies that their intent was wonder and inquiry, not disparagement.
The key is distinguishing intent.
4
Understand the third objection (Harut and Marut) and its answer
The third objection claims Harut and Marut were punished for sorcery. The answer states they did not commit sorcery or believe in it; they only taught it as a trial, and their punishment is a reproach like that of prophets.
This emphasizes the temporary nature of their trial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسك القائلون بالعصمة بمثل قوله تعالى : وهم لا يستكبرون . بخافون ربهم من فوقهم ويفعلون ما يؤمرون . . . يسبحون الليل والنهار لا يفترون . والمخالفون بأن إبليس مع كونه من الملائكة أبى واستكبر وكان من الكافرين ، وبأن قول الملائكة أتجعل فيها من يفسد فيها . . الآية اغتياب للخليفة، واستبعاد لفعل الله تعالى وإعجاب بأنفسهم، وبأن هاروت وماروت يعذبان لارتكابهما السحر . والجواب : أن إبليس من الجن وعد من الملائكة تغليباً، وأن الاغتياب والإعجاب إنما هو حيث يكون الغرض منقصة الغير ومنقبة النفس، وإنما غرضهم التعجب والاستفسار عن حكمة استخلاف من لا يليق به مع وجود اللائق، وأن هاروت وماروت لم يكونا مرتكبين للسحر، ولا معتقدين لتأثيره، وإنما أنزل عليهما السحر ابتلاء للناس، وكانا يعلمان ويعظان ويقولان : ( إنما نحن فتنة ) وتعذيبهما معاتبة كما يعاتب الأنبياء .
The proponents of infallibility adduced such as His saying, the Exalted: 'And they are not arrogant. They fear their Lord above them and do what they are commanded... They glorify [Him] night and day, never flagging.' And the opponents [adduced] that Iblis, despite being one of the angels, refused and was arrogant and became one of the disbelievers, and that the saying of the angels 'Will You place therein one who causes corruption therein...' [is] backbiting of the caliph, and deeming God's act unlikely, and self-admiration, and that Harut and Marut are punished for their commission of sorcery. And the answer: that Iblis is of the jinn and was counted among the angels by way of predominant classification (taghlīban), and that backbiting and self-admiration only occur where the intent is disparagement of another and self-praise, while their intent was merely wonder and inquiry about the wisdom of appointing as caliph one who is not suitable when there exists one who is suitable, and that Harut and Marut were not committing sorcery nor believing in its effect, but sorcery was sent down to them as a trial for the people, and they were teaching and admonishing and saying, 'We are but a trial (fitna),' and their punishment is a reproach just as the prophets are reproached.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents a complete objection-and-answer argument regarding the infallibility of angels. First, it gives the proof texts of those who affirm infallibility (al-qā'ilūn bi-l-'iṣma), citing Quranic verses that describe angels as not arrogant, fearing God, obeying commands, and glorifying Him ceaselessly. Then it lists three objections from the opponents: (1) Iblis (Satan) was an angel and yet disobeyed and became a disbeliever; (2) the angels' question in Surah al-Baqara about God placing a caliph who causes corruption constitutes backbiting, deeming God's act unlikely, and self-admiration; (3) the angels Harut and Marut were punished for practicing sorcery. The answer addresses each: (1) Iblis is actually from the jinn but was included in the address to angels by way of predominant classification (taghlīb); (2) the angels' question was not backbiting or self-admiration but was an expression of wonder and a request for wisdom; (3) Harut and Marut did not actually commit sorcery or believe in its effect; rather, sorcery was sent to them as a trial, and they warned people. Their punishment is a form of reproach (mu'ātaba) like that given to prophets.

Key Terms
عصمة
إبليس
هاروت وماروت
تغليب
فتنة
معاتبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Iblis was an angel by essence, and that his disobedience proves angels are not infallible.
Correction: The answer clarifies that Iblis is from the jinn, not an angel by nature, and was only counted among the angels by predominance (taghlīb).

Checkpoint

A) Iblis repented after his sin.
B) Iblis was not an angel by nature but from the jinn.
C) Iblis was only a test for the angels.
D) Iblis was forced to disobey.

Key Takeaway
The apparent contradictions to angelic infallibility are resolved by showing that Iblis is not an angel by nature, the angels' question was not backbiting, and Harut and Marut did not commit sorcery.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you encounter something in God's creation or decree that seems puzzling or even problematic—like the angels questioning the caliph—do you react with humble wonder and a desire to understand wisdom, or do you let doubt and criticism creep in? Which response dominates your heart right now, and what one change can you make to cultivate more trust?
?
The angels Harut and Marut were sent as a trial, yet they remained obedient and warned others. In your life, are there situations where you are a 'trial' for others (e.g., through your example or words)? Do you handle that responsibility with the same care, realizing that your actions may be a test for someone else? What will you do differently tomorrow to be a better 'trial'?

Four Proofs for the Superiority of Prophets over Angels
Enumerates four arguments for the superiority of prophets over angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four arguments used to support the superiority of prophets over angels, as presented in classical Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Four proofs from scripture and reason establish the prophets' rank above the angels.

This lesson presents four proofs (wujūh) put forward by scholars who affirm that prophets are superior to angels. The proofs draw from Quranic verses and rational arguments, addressing the prostration of angels to Adam, the teaching of the names, divine election, and the nature of obedience amidst trials. Understanding these arguments is essential for grasping a key theological position in Ash'ari and Shi'i thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get an overview. Notice the four numbered proofs (الأول, الثاني, الثالث, الرابع).
2
Identify each proof's basis
For each proof, determine whether it is scriptural (Quranic event) or rational. The first three are scriptural; the fourth is rational.
The first proof references the prostration event, the second the teaching of names, the third the verse of election, and the fourth is a rational argument about reward.
3
Analyze the first proof in depth
Focus on the nature of the prostration: it was a prostration of the lower to the higher, indicating superiority. Note the evidence from Iblis's refusal.
Iblis's argument 'I am better than him' presupposes that prostration implies the prostrator is lower.
4
Reflect on the fourth proof
Consider why persisting in obedience despite distractions is more meritorious. Prophets face more trials and yet remain steadfast, increasing their reward and rank.
This argument uses the concept of greater effort leading to greater reward.
5
Synthesize the overall argument
Combine all four proofs to understand how they collectively build the case for prophets' superiority over angels.
The proofs cover divine commands, knowledge, divine choice, and moral struggle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتمسك القائلون بفضل الأنبياء وهم جمهور أصحابنا والشيعة بوجوه : الأول: أمر الملائكة بالسجود لآدم سجدة الأدنى للأعلى تعظيماً وتكرمة، لا زيارة وتحية، بدليل استكبار إبليس وتعليله بأنه خير منه لكونه من نار، وآدم من طين . الثاني - أمر آدم بتعليمهم الأسماء قصداً إلى إظهار فضله . الثالث - أن الله اصطفى آدم ونوحاً وآل إبراهيم وآل عمران على العالمين، الذين من جملتهم الملائكة . الرابع - ان المواظبة على الطاعات مع الشواغل واكتساب الكمال مع العوائق أدخل في استحقاق الثواب .
And the proponents of the superiority of the prophets—and they are the majority of our companions (aṣḥābunā) and the Shī'a—adduced several aspects: First: The command to the angels to prostrate to Adam as a prostration of the lower to the higher for veneration and honor, not as a greeting or salutation, as evidenced by Iblis's arrogance and his reasoning that he is better than him because he was created from fire and Adam from clay. Second: God's command to Adam to teach them the names, intending to manifest his virtue. Third: That God chose Adam, Noah, the family of Abraham, and the family of ʿImrān over the worlds, who include the angels. Fourth: That persistence in acts of obedience despite distractions and acquiring perfection despite obstacles is more deserving of reward.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage enumerates four proofs (wujūh) given by those who hold that prophets are superior to angels—the majority of the author's school (the Ashʿarīs? 'our companions' likely refers to the Ashʿarīs) and the Shīʿa. The first proof: God commanded the angels to prostrate to Adam; this prostration was one of the lower to the higher (i.e., the angel prostrated to the prophet), indicating Adam's superiority, as Iblis's refusal shows he understood that prostration implies superiority. The second proof: God had Adam teach the angels the names, demonstrating Adam's knowledge and virtue. The third proof: God chose Adam and other prophets over 'the worlds,' which includes the angels. The fourth proof: Persisting in obedience and acquiring perfection despite worldly distractions and obstacles (which prophets face more than angels) makes one more deserving of reward, implying a higher rank.

Key Terms
فضل الأنبياء
سجود
تعليم الأسماء
اصطفاء
المواظبة على الطاعات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the angels' prostration to Adam was an act of worship (like praying to him).
Correction: The passage clarifies it was a prostration of veneration for a higher being, not worship.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof mentioned, what indicates the superiority of prophets over angels?
A) The prophets performed more miracles.
B) God chose Adam and other prophets over the worlds, which include angels.
C) The angels cannot acquire perfection.
D) The prophets were created before the angels.

Key Takeaway
The four proofs from divine command, knowledge, election, and perseverance establish that prophets hold a higher rank than angels.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The fourth proof teaches that persisting in obedience despite distractions earns a higher reward. What is one distraction in your daily life that you use as an excuse to neglect prayer or Qur’an? Will you commit this week to turning that distraction into a moment of worship?
?
If angels—sinless beings—were commanded to honor Adam by prostrating, what does that teach you about your own pride? In which relationship (parent, spouse, leader) has Allah placed someone you struggle to respect for His sake? What will you do today to lower your ego and honor them?
?
God manifested Adam’s virtue by having him teach the angels. What gift or knowledge has Allah given you that you are hiding out of fear or laziness? What is one step you can take this week to teach it to others for His sake?

The Nature of Angels and Two Disputes
Consensus on angelic nature and introduction of two disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus among Muslims regarding the nature of angels and the two main theological disputes about them.

Core Idea
Angels are subtle, honored servants; two disputes remain on infallibility and superiority.

Before diving into detailed arguments, the author sets the stage by outlining what Muslims agree on about angels and what they disagree on. This passage establishes the shared foundation about angelic nature and identifies the two key disputes (infallibility and superiority over prophets) that will be debated. Understanding this framework is essential before examining the proofs from each side.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall flow. Notice that the passage moves from consensus to dispute to the author's plan.
The passage is dense; take your time to absorb each attribute mentioned.
2
Identify the consensus points about angels
List the attributes that the majority of Muslims agree upon: subtle bodies, appearing in various forms, capability for difficult actions, honored servants, persistent obedience, and no gender.
These are the foundational beliefs that are not contested.
3
Spot the two disputes
Find the two issues where disagreement exists: infallibility (عصمة) and superiority over prophets (فضل). Note that the author says neither side has a decisive argument.
These disputes will be elaborated in the following lessons.
4
Understand the author's plan
The author announces he will present the arguments of both sides, starting with the first station: infallibility. This sets up the structure for the subsequent discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جمهور المسلمين على أن الملائكة أجسام لطيفة تظهر في صور مختلفة ، وتقوى على أفعال شاقة، هم عباد مكرمون يواظبون على الطاعة والعبادة، ولا يوصفون بالذكورة والأنوثة، واستقر الخلاف بين المسلمين في عصمتهم، وفي فضلهم على الأنبياء، ولا قاطع في أحد الجانبين، فلنذكر تمسكات الفريقين في المقامين : المقام الأول - أعني العصمة
The majority of Muslims agree that angels are subtle bodies that appear in various forms, and are capable of difficult actions. They are honored servants who persist in obedience and worship, and are not described with masculinity or femininity. And the dispute among Muslims settled on their infallibility and on their superiority over prophets, and there is no decisive argument on either side. So let us mention the arguments of the two groups in the two stations: The first station – meaning infallibility.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage first describes the consensus view on angels: they are subtle bodies capable of assuming different forms and performing strenuous tasks, they are devoted servants of God, and they are not gendered. Then it identifies the two theological disputes regarding angels: (1) their infallibility (ismah) and (2) their superiority over prophets. The author notes that neither side has a decisive proof. He then announces that he will present the arguments of both sides, beginning with the first dispute, which is infallibility.

Misconception: Angels are purely spiritual and have no physical form whatsoever.
Correction: The passage states angels are 'subtle bodies' (أجسام لطيفة), meaning they have a form but it is fine and non-material like ordinary bodies. They can appear in different shapes.

Checkpoint

A) Their creation from light
B) Their infallibility
C) Their number
D) Their names

Key Takeaway
Muslims agree that angels are subtle, powerful, obedient, and genderless beings, but they disagree on whether angels are infallible and whether they are superior to prophets.

Quranic Proofs for Angelic Infallibility
The author cites Quranic verses supporting angelic infallibility and discusses certainty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verses cited by theologians support angelic infallibility on a probabilistic level, and why this is considered valid evidence in matters of belief.

Core Idea
The Quran gives strong probability for angelic infallibility, and probability matters in creed even without certainty.

In this passage, the author presents the scriptural case for angelic infallibility. He cites three verses that emphasize angelic obedience and worship, then addresses a potential objection about the value of probability in matters of belief. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and clarifies the role of probabilistic evidence in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage with its translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice the verses cited and the logical flow.
2
Identify the three verses
List the three Quranic verses and what they collectively describe about angels.
The verses are from Surahs al-Nahl, al-Anbiya', and al-Anbiya' (or similar).
3
Understand probability vs certainty
The author notes these verses yield probability (zann) not certainty (yaqin). Understand this distinction.
In kalam, zann is acceptable for some proofs, but definitive matters require yaqin.
4
Analyze the objection and reply
The author responds to the claim that probability is irrelevant. Distinguish between what he concedes and what he denies.
He concedes that probability does not give firm conviction, but denies it gives no belief at all.
5
Reflect on methodology
Consider why this argument is important for kalam: it shows that even non-definitive texts have weight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتمسك المثبتون بمثل قوله تعالى : ( وهم لا يستكبرون . يخافون ربهم من فوقهم ويفعلون ما يؤمرون) وقوله تعالى: بل عباد مكرمون . لا يسبقونه بالقول وهم بأمره يعملون .. إلى قوله : وهم من خشيته مشفقون ) وقوله تعالى : لا يستكبرون عن عبادته ولا يستحسرون . يسبحون الليل والنهار لا يفترون . ولا خفاء في أن أمثال هذه العمومات تفيد الظن وإن لم تفد اليقين، وما يقال أنه لا عبرة بالظنيات في باب الاعتقادات فإن أريد أنه لا يحصل منه الاعتقاد الجازم ولا يصح الحكم القطعي، فلا نزاع فيه. وإن أريد أنه لا يحصل الظن بذلك الحكم فظاهر البطلان .
So those who affirm (infallibility) hold by such verses as His statement: 'And they are not arrogant. They fear their Lord above them and do what they are commanded.' And His statement: 'Rather, they are honored servants. They do not precede Him in speech, and they act by His command... up to His statement: 'And they are wary of His fear.'' And His statement: 'They are not arrogant in worship nor do they tire. They glorify Him night and day, never slackening.' And it is not hidden that such general statements yield probability even if they do not yield certainty. And as for what is said that probabilities have no weight in matters of belief – if it is meant that a firm belief is not obtained and a definitive judgment is not valid, there is no dispute about that. But if it is meant that probability of that judgment is not obtained, that is clearly false.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents the arguments of those who affirm angelic infallibility. They cite three Quranic verses that describe angels as obedient, not arrogant, and constantly worshiping. The author notes that these general verses provide strong probability (zann) for infallibility, but not certainty (yaqin). He then addresses a potential objection that probability is insufficient in matters of belief. He clarifies: if the objection means that such verses cannot produce firm conviction or definitive judgment, then that is true. But if it means they cannot produce even probable belief, then that is clearly false. Thus the verses carry weight as probabilistic evidence.

Key Terms

مثبتون
عمومات
ظن
يقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because probabilities don't yield certainty in creed, they are completely irrelevant.
Correction: The author clarifies that while probability does not produce definitive judgment, it still yields probable belief, which is not to be dismissed.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the claim that probabilities are not considered in matters of belief?
A) He agrees entirely
B) He says it is false because probability leads to certainty
C) He distinguishes between producing firm conviction and producing probable belief
D) He says the verses give certainty

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verses about angels provide strong probability (zann) for their infallibility, and such probability carries weight in theological arguments even if it falls short of certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The author says these verses give only probability, not certainty. Are you comfortable living with probability in your faith? Where in your life do you demand certainty from God when He has only given probability? How does that affect your trust in His revelation?
?
If you were to be as careful about sin as the angels who are 'wary of His fear,' what is one habit you would change starting today? What prevents you from making that change right now?

First Objection to Angelic Infallibility: The Case of Iblis
The author presents the objection that Iblis was an angel who disobeyed, and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection of Iblis's disobedience is raised and refuted in classical Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Iblis was a jinn, not an angel – a key refutation against denying angelic infallibility.

This passage presents the first objection raised by those who deny that angels are infallible. They point to the example of Iblis (Satan) who disobeyed God's command to prostrate. The author then refutes this by arguing that Iblis was not an angel but a jinn, and his inclusion among angels was by way of predominance (taghlib).

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first part of the passage to find the three pieces of evidence the deniers use to argue that Iblis was an angel.
Look for the Quranic verses cited.
2
Understand the refutation
The author replies by denying that Iblis was an angel. Note the key claim: he was from the jinn, and the concept of taghlib (predominance) is introduced.
Taghlib means including a minority under the majority term.
3
Examine the counter-argument
The author anticipates a possible objection: some might interpret 'from the jinn' as a type of angel. Identify this counter-argument.
4
Note the author's final response
The author rejects the counter-interpretation as weak in transmission and contrary to the apparent meaning of the Quran.
This shows the importance of sound transmission in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسك النافون بوجوه : الأول - أن إبليس مع كونه من الملائكة بدليل تناول أمر الملائكة بالسجود في قوله تعالى : ( وإذ قلنا للملائكة اسجدوا لآدم ) إياه . ولذا عوتب بقوله تعالى: ما منعك ألا تسجد إذ أمرتك) . وببدليل صحة استثنائه منهم في قوله تعالى : فسجدوا إلا إبليس )) وقوله تعالى : ( فسجد الملائكة كلهم أجمعون إلا إبليس أبي أن يكون مع الساجدين ) . ورد بالمنع بل كان من الجن ففسق عن أمر ربه وإنما أدرج في الملائكة على سبيل التغليب لكونه جنياً واحداً مغموراً فيما بينهم، لا يقال معنى قوله : «كان من الجن صار أو كان طائفة من من الملائكة مسماة بالجن، شأنهم الاستكبار، لانا نقول : هذا مع كونه كلاماً على السند خلاف الظاهر.
Those who deny (infallibility) hold by several arguments. The first: That Iblis, despite being from the angels, by evidence of the command to the angels to prostrate in His statement: 'And when We said to the angels, "Prostrate to Adam"' including him. Therefore he was rebuked by His statement: 'What prevented you from prostrating when I commanded you?' And by evidence of the validity of his exception from them in His statement: 'So they prostrated except Iblis' and His statement: 'So the angels prostrated, all of them together, except Iblis; he refused to be among those prostrating.' It is replied with denial: rather he was from the jinn and transgressed against his Lord's command. He was only included among the angels by way of predominance (taghlib) because he was a single jinn submerged among them. It cannot be said: the meaning of 'he was from the jinn' is that he became or that he was a group of angels called jinn, whose characteristic is arrogance. For we say: this, besides being a statement against the chain (of transmission), is contrary to the apparent meaning.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection of those who deny angelic infallibility. They argue that Iblis (Satan) was an angel based on Quranic evidence: the command to the angels to prostrate included him, and later he was rebuked for not prostrating; also he is excepted from the angels in two verses. Thus an angel disobeyed, contradicting infallibility. The answer (refutation) is that Iblis was not an angel but a jinn. He was only included under the term 'angels' by way of predominance (taghlib) because he was a single jinn among many angels. The author then addresses a potential counterargument: some might claim that the phrase 'he was from the jinn' means he became a jinn or that there is a group of angels called jinn who are characterized by arrogance. The author rejects this by saying it is a statement against the chain (i.e., weak transmission) and contradicts the apparent meaning of the Quran.

Key Terms
إبليس
عصمة
نافون
جن
تغليب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection proves that angels can sin because Iblis disobeyed.
Correction: The refutation shows Iblis was not an angel, so the objection does not apply.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why was Iblis not an angel?
A) He was a jinn included by predominance
B) He was a fallen angel
C) He was a human
D) There is no answer

Key Takeaway
The objection using Iblis is refuted by establishing that Iblis was a jinn, not an angel, and his inclusion among angels was by predominance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used a single counterexample to deny a clear truth taught by revelation? What does this passage teach you about the importance of proper interpretation and not jumping to conclusions?
?
Are there areas in your life where you take Quranic verses out of context to justify a flawed belief or action? What steps can you take to ensure you understand the Quran as the scholars do?
?
The refutation uses taghlib—judging by the majority appearance. Where in your life do you judge based on majority appearance rather than investigating the reality? How can you protect yourself from this error?

The Angels' Question: Objection and Refutation
Objection and refutation regarding the angels' question

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the angels' question about Adam does not contradict their infallibility, and how the response refutes the accusation of backbiting and self-praise.

Core Idea
The angels' question was a permissible inquiry, not disobedience.

The second objection against angelic infallibility is based on the angels' question in the Quran: 'Will You place upon it one who will cause corruption...?' At first glance, this seems to involve backbiting and self-praise. But the scholars explain that the angels' intent was not to criticize God's wisdom but to seek understanding. In this passage, the author presents the objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage that lists the charges: backbiting, improbabilizing God's act, following conjecture, self-praise. Notice how each seems to violate infallibility.
2
Read the answer
Read the refutation starting with 'And the answer'. Note how it distinguishes the intentions necessary for backbiting and self-praise.
The key point: intent matters.
3
Identify the three sources of their knowledge
The passage mentions three ways the angels could have known about future corruption: divine notification, the Preserved Tablet, or analogy with jinn. Understand that their question was based on knowledge, not assumption.
4
Reflect on the lesson for infallibility
Consider how this answer preserves the angels' infallibility. Their question was a permissible act of seeking wisdom, not a sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - أن قولهم في جواب : إني جاعل في الأرض خليفة : ( أتجعل فيها من يفسد فيها ويسفك الدماء ونحن نسبح بحمدك ونقدس لك ) غتياب للخليفة واستبعاد لفعل الله تعالى بحيث يشبه صورة الإنكار بمعنى أنه لا ينبغي أن يكون، واتباع للظن، ورجم بالغيب فيما لا يليق وإعجاب بأنفسهم، وتزكية .لها. وأمثال هذه تخل بالعصمة لا محالة. والجواب : ان الاغتياب إنما يكون حيث الغرض إظهار منقصة الغير، والتزكية حيث الغرض إظهار منقبة النفس. ولا يتصور ذلك بالنسبة إلى علام الغيوب، بل الغرض التعجب والاستفسار عن حكمة استخلاف من يتصف بما لا يليق بذلك، مع وجود الأولى والأليق . وإنما علموا ذلك بإعلام من الله تعالى، أو مشاهدة من اللوح، أو مقايسة بين الجن والإنس بمشاركتهما في الشهوة والغضب المفضيين إلى الفساد وسفك الدماء.
Second: That their statement in response to 'I am placing a vicegerent on the earth': 'Will You place upon it one who will cause corruption and shed blood while we glorify You with praise and sanctify You?' is backbiting of the vicegerent, an improbabilizing of God's act such that it resembles a form of denial meaning that it should not be, and following conjecture, and shooting into the unseen in a manner not fitting, and self-admiration, and self-praise. Such things necessarily detract from infallibility. And the answer: Backbiting only occurs when the intent is to show a defect in another, and self-praise when the intent is to show a virtue in oneself. That is not conceivable with respect to the Knower of the Unseen. Rather, the intent was wonder and inquiry about the wisdom of appointing a vicegerent characterized by what is not fitting given the presence of what is better and more suitable. And they only knew that by a notification from God, or by observation from the Preserved Tablet, or by analogy between jinn and humans due to their sharing in desire and anger which lead to corruption and bloodshed.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection: the angels' question in the Quran about God creating a vicegerent who would cause corruption seems to involve backbiting of the future humans, questioning God's wisdom, self-praise, and reliance on conjecture—all of which contradict infallibility. The answer refutes each point: backbiting and self-praise require intentions that are impossible towards God, who knows all. The angels were not criticizing but asking out of wonder and seeking wisdom. They had knowledge of such corruption either through divine notification, the Preserved Tablet, or analogy with the jinn who previously corrupted. Thus their question was not sinful.

Key Terms
اغتياب
تزكية
الملائكة
استخلاف
عصمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The angels sinned by questioning God's wisdom and backbiting the future humans.
Correction: The angels did not sin because their intent was not to backbite or self-praise; they sought wisdom. They had knowledge of future corruption, so their question was based on known facts, not guesswork.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, the angels' question was not backbiting because:
A) They did not intend to show a defect
B) They were commanded to ask
C) They were misguided
D) The text is unclear

Key Takeaway
The angels' question was a permissible inquiry based on known facts, not a sinful act of backbiting or self-praise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see wrongdoing in the world, do your thoughts ever carry a tone of superiority or self-righteousness, like the misconstrued objection of the angels? How can you transform that into humble inquiry before God?
?
Have you ever questioned a divine decree in your own life, only to realize later that your objection was rooted in self-admiration or limited knowledge? What does this lesson teach you about the need to purify your intentions when seeking wisdom?
?
The angels' question was based on knowledge given by God, yet still needed to be framed as inquiry, not criticism. When you discuss the faults of others, are you careful to check your intention — are you seeking to understand God's wisdom, or subtly elevating yourself?

The Prophet's Denial of Being an Angel: An Objection Answered
Presents an objection from Quranic verse and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a Quranic verse used to argue for angels' superiority is refuted by clarifying its intended meaning regarding specific abilities rather than rank.

Core Idea
The verse proves a difference in ability, not superiority in rank.

Scholars debating the superiority of angels over prophets sometimes cite the Quranic verse where the Prophet (ﷺ) says he is not an angel. This lesson examines that objection and its rebuttal, showing that the verse addresses specific powers, not overall rank.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its source verse
Begin by reading the objection: the verse where the Prophet denies being an angel. Understand why this could be interpreted as angels being superior.
The verse is from Surah Al-An'am (6:50).
2
Study the refutation's reasoning
The answer explains that the verse means the Prophet lacks angelic powers like directly bringing punishment or receiving revelation without an intermediary. This is about ability, not rank.
The refutation uses the example of Gabriel's power.
3
Distinguish between ability and rank
Reflect on the difference between having a specific power and being superior overall. The Prophet's statement does not diminish his status; it only clarifies his role.
This distinction is key in the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - قوله تعالى : قل لا أقول لكم عندي خزائن الله ولا أعلم الغيب ولا أقول إني ملك) (۱) وأجيب بأن المعنى لست بملك حتى يكون لي القوة والقدرة على إنزال العذاب بإذن الله كما كان بجبريل ، أو يكون لي العلم بذلك بإخبار الله تعالى بلا واسطة .
The second: His saying, exalted: 'Say, I do not say to you that I possess the treasuries of God, nor do I know the unseen, nor do I say that I am an angel' (6:50). And it was answered that the meaning is: I am not an angel such that I would have the strength and power to send down punishment by God's permission as Gabriel had, or that I would have knowledge of that by God's informing without intermediary.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the superiority of angels over prophets. The objection cites the verse where the Prophet (ﷺ) says he is not an angel, implying angels are superior. The answer (وأجيب) explains that the verse means the Prophet is not an angel with the power to bring punishment directly, not that angels are superior in rank. It distinguishes between having power and being superior.

Key Terms
الملائكة
الأنبياء
الأفضلية
جبريل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic verse (6:50) proves that angels are superior to prophets because the Prophet denies being an angel.
Correction: The verse only indicates that the Prophet lacks certain angelic powers (like directly inflicting punishment or knowing the unseen without revelation), not that angels are higher in rank. The refutation shows the verse is about functional differences, not superiority.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the answer to the second objection?
A) The Prophet is not an angel, so angels are superior.
B) The Prophet denied having the power to punish directly, not superiority.
C) Angels cannot be compared to prophets.
D) The verse is not relevant.

Key Takeaway
The verse does not establish angels' superiority; it merely differentiates roles and abilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Are there areas of your life where you quietly resent your human limitations—lack of knowledge, inability to control outcomes—and wish for angelic abilities? How does this lesson challenge that resentment?
?

Third Objection: Temptation to Become Angels
Objection using Adam and Eve's temptation to become angels, and refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against the superiority of prophets over angels and its refutation based on limiting the indication to before prophethood.

Core Idea
The verse only suggests superiority before prophethood, not after.

The third objection draws from the story of Adam and Eve, where Satan tempts them by claiming that Allah forbade the tree only to prevent them from becoming angels. This seems to imply that being an angel is superior. But the refutation clarifies a critical limitation: the superiority indicated is only before Adam received prophethood, thus not undermining the final superiority of prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: the objection is introduced as 'الثالث' followed by the verse quote, then the answer begins with 'وأجيب'.
The word 'تخييلاً' means 'deception' or 'illusion'.
2
Distinguish the objection and the answer
Identify which part is the objection (the verse from Satan's speech) and which part is the theological answer. The answer is what the theologian adds to refute the objection.
3
Analyze the answer's limitation
The answer concedes that the verse could indicate superiority, but only over Adam before his prophethood. Why is this distinction important? Reflect on how prophethood changes a person's status.
Prophetic status is considered higher than angelic status in Islamic theology.
4
Connect to the larger debate
Consider how this refutation weakens the argument that angels are superior to prophets. The objection only works if the comparison is made before prophethood, which is irrelevant to the final hierarchy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - ما نهاكما ربكما عن هذه الشجرة إلا أن تكونا ملكين ) (٢) وأجيب بأنه مع كونه تخييلاً من الشيطان إنما يفيد الأفضلية على آدم قبل البعثة .
The third: 'Your Lord forbade you not this tree except that you become angels' (7:20). And it was answered that, despite it being a deception from Satan, it only indicates superiority over Adam before his prophethood.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage contains the third objection arguing for angels' superiority, citing the verse where Satan tempts Adam and Eve by suggesting they would become angels. The answer (وأجيب) states that even though this is a deception from Satan, it only shows that becoming an angel would make one superior to Adam before he became a prophet, not after prophethood. Thus it does not prove the absolute superiority of angels over prophets.

Key Terms
آدم
الشيطان
الأفضلية
البعثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse implies that angels are absolutely superior to Adam (and thus to all prophets).
Correction: The answer limits the indication to superiority only before Adam's prophethood, making it irrelevant to the debate over the superiority of prophets after their prophethood.

Checkpoint
What does the answer say about the verse's indication of angels' superiority?
A) It proves angels are superior to prophets.
B) It only shows superiority over Adam before his prophethood.
C) It is a true statement from Satan.
D) It is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The verse does not prove angels' absolute superiority over prophets because its indication is limited to before Adam's prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Satan used a deceptive argument that contained a partial truth. When in your life have you accepted a half-truth because it seemed beneficial? How will you guard against such deception after learning this lesson?
?
Adam's status was elevated after prophethood. What are you doing today to elevate your rank with Allah, or are you content with your current state? What one action will you take this week to seek nearness to Him?
?
This lesson shows that even a Qur'anic verse can be misused if context is ignored. Are you careful to understand Islamic teachings in their full context, or do you sometimes take verses out of convenience? Commit to studying one verse with its tafsir before the end of the week.
