، فقال بعضهم : يمتنع إعادتها مطلقاً، لأن المعاد إنما يعاد بمعنى فيلزم قيام المعنى بالمعنى . وإلى هذا ذهب بعض أصحابنا . وقال الأكثرون منهم بامتناع إعادة الاعراض التي لا تبقى كالأصوات والإرادات لاختصاصها عندهم بالأوقات، وقسموا الباقية الى ما يكون مقدوراً للعبد، وحكموا بأنه لا يجوز إعادتها، لا للعبد ولا للرب، وإلى ما لا يكون مقدوراً للعبد، وجوزوا إعادتها لنا إقناعاً أن الأصل فيما لا دليل على وجوبه وامتناعه هو الإمكان على ما قالت الحكماء أن كل ما قرع سمعك من الغرائب فذره في بقعة الامكان ما لم يزدك قائم البرهان. فمن ادعى عدم إعادة المعدوم فعليه الدليل، وإلزاماً أن المعاد مثل المبدأ، بل عينه لأن الكلام في إعادة المعدوم بعينه، ويستحيل كون الشيء ممكناً في وقت ممتنعاً في وقت للقطع بأنه لا أثر للأوقات فيما هو بالذات . وعلى هذا لا يرد ما يقال أن العود ، وهو الوجود ثانياً أخص من مطلق الوجود، ولا يلزم من إمكان الأعم إمكان الأخص. وقريب من هذا ما يقال أن المعدوم الممكن قابل للوجود ضرورة استحالة الانقلاب فالوجود الأول أن إفادة زيادة استعداد لقبول الوجود على ما هو شأن سائر القوابل، بناء على اكتساب ملكة الاتصاف بالفعل، فقد صار قابليته للوجود ثانياً أقرب وإعادته على الفاعل أهون، ويشبه أن يكون هذا هو الحق. والمراد بقوله تعالى : وهو الذي يبدأ الخلق ثم يعيده وهو أهون عليه ) (۱) وإن لم يفده زيادة الاستعداد فمعلوم بالضرورة أنه لا عليه بالذات من قابلية الوجود في جميع الأوقات. هذا ولكن الأقرب أن تحمل الإعادة التي جعلت أهون على إعادة الأجزاء ومـا تفتت من المواد إلى ما كانت عليه من الصور والتأليفات على ما يشير إليه قوله تعالى : قل يحييها الذي أنشأها أول مرة ) (۲) لا على إعادة المعدوم، لأنه لم يبق
He said: The First Investigation – It is permissible to re-create the non-existent, contrary to the philosophers absolutely, and contrary to some Mu'tazila regarding accidents, and contrary to some of them regarding what does not remain among them, like sounds. For us, as an inducement (iqna'an), [we say] that the original principle is possibility (al-imkan) until a proof of necessity or impossibility arises, and as a compulsion (ilzaman) that the return (al-ma'ad) is like the beginning (al-mabda'), nay, it is the very same thing, so it is impossible for it to be possible at one time and impossible at another time. Rather, perhaps it is claimed that the first existence confers an increase of preparedness for receiving existence, as indicated by His saying, exalted is He, 'and it is easier for Him' (wa huwa ahwanu 'alayhi) – and there is a consideration in this. It cannot be said: perhaps it is impossible due to a necessary matter, for we say: either it is impossible or not. Many of the investigations of the theologians seem outwardly alien to the knowledge of religious beliefs, but when the original objectives are verified, it is known that they are beneficial in presenting proofs for them or repelling doubts about them. That includes re-creating the non‑existent, the subsistence of the atom, the void, the validity of annihilation for the world, the permissibility of breaking through the celestial spheres, the non‑conditionality of life by the body, the non‑necessity of the finitude of physical powers, and the like, in proving the Resurrection, the punishment of the grave, and eternal life in Paradise or Hell… and other things according to the diversity of opinions. He only delayed the discussion of re‑creating the non‑existent specifically to this place because of its special relevance to the matter of the Return (al‑ma'ad), since it is not needed except in proving the Return by way of existence after annihilation. The majority of theologians agreed on its permissibility, and the philosophers on its impossibility. As for the Mu'tazila, Basran sect held the permissibility of re‑creating substances, but based on the persistence of their essences in non‑existence, so that if they were annihilated, their re‑creation would be impossible. They differed regarding accidents. Some of them said: their re‑creation is absolutely impossible because the re‑created thing (al-mu'ad) is only re‑created by a meaning (bi-ma'nan), so it would entail the subsistence of a meaning by a meaning. Some of our companions (Ash'aris) held this view. The majority of them said: it is impossible to re‑create those accidents that do not persist, like sounds and wills, due to their specificity to times according to them. They divided the persisting accidents into what is within the ability of the servant, and ruled that it is not permissible to re‑create them, neither for the servant nor for the Lord; and into what is not within the ability of the servant, and they permitted its re‑creation. For us, as an inducement (iqna'an), [we say] that the original principle regarding what there is no proof of its necessity or impossibility is possibility (al-imkan), as the philosophers said: 'Whatever strange thing reaches your ears, cast it into the realm of possibility as long as a clear proof does not prevent you.' So whoever claims the impossibility of re‑creating the non‑existent, the burden of proof is on him. And as a compulsion (ilzaman), that the return (al-ma'ad) is like the beginning (al-mabda'), nay, it is the very same thing, because the discussion is about re‑creating the very same non‑existent. It is impossible for a thing to be possible at one time and impossible at another, because it is certain that times have no effect on what is in itself [possible]. On this basis, what is said – that the return (al-'awd), which is existence a second time, is more specific than absolute existence, and the possibility of the more general does not entail the possibility of the more specific – is not valid. Close to this is what is said: the non‑existent possible is receptive to existence by the necessity of the impossibility of conversion; the first existence confers an increase of preparedness for receiving existence, as is the case of all recipients, based on acquiring a disposition for being qualified by the act. So its receptivity for existence a second time becomes closer, and its re‑creation is easier for the Agent. And this seems to be the truth. What is meant by His saying, exalted is He: 'It is He who begins creation, then repeats it, and it is easier for Him' (al‑Rum 30:27) – even if it does not confer an increase of preparedness, yet it is known necessarily that there is no inherent impossibility in its receptivity to existence at all times. But the closest [interpretation] is to carry the re‑creation that is made easier upon the re‑creation of the parts and what has become disintegrated from the materials into what they were of forms and structures, as indicated by His saying, exalted is He: 'Say: He will give them life who created them the first time' (Ya Sin 36:79), not upon re‑creating the non‑existent, because it has not remained.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents the first investigation (al-mabhas al-awwal) of the chapter on al-ma'ad, concerning the permissibility of re-creating the non-existent (i'adat al-ma'dum). The author begins by stating the position that it is permissible, contrary to the philosophers who deny it absolutely, and contrary to some Mu'tazila who restrict the denial to accidents or to non‑persistent accidents like sounds. He then gives two arguments: first, an inducement (iqna') that the default judgment for anything is possibility (al-imkan) until a proof establishes necessity or impossibility; second, a compulsion (ilzam) that the return (al-ma'ad) is like the beginning (al-mabda')—indeed the very same entity—so it cannot be possible at one time and impossible at another, since times have no effect on essential possibility. The author notes a possible claim that the first existence confers an increase of preparedness, citing the verse 'and it is easier for Him'. He then raises a consideration: an objection might be that the thing is impossible due to a necessary concomitant, but the response is that either that concomitant renders it impossible or not; if not, the objection fails. Following this, the author gives a methodological note explaining that many theological discussions appear unrelated to creed but are actually useful for defending doctrines. He delays the discussion of i'adat al-ma'dum to this chapter because it is specifically needed for proving the Resurrection as existence after annihilation. He then reports that the majority of theologians (mutakallimun) affirm permissibility, while philosophers (hukama') deny it. The Mu'tazila are divided: the Basran school allows re‑creation of substances based on persistence of essences in non‑existence, but differs on accidents. One group absolutely denies re‑creation of accidents on the ground that the re‑created thing would be re‑created by a meaning, leading to an infinite regress; some Ash'aris hold this. The majority deny re‑creation of non‑persistent accidents (sounds, wills) due to their time‑specificity, and for persistent accidents they distinguish between what is within human power (which cannot be re‑created by anyone) and what is not (which may be re‑created). The author then returns to his own school's proofs: the iqna' argument based on the principle of possibility (quoting the philosophers' maxim), and the ilzam argument that the return is identical to the beginning. He refutes the objection that 'return' (existence a second time) is more specific than absolute existence, so possibility of the general does not entail possibility of the specific. He also discusses a related point: the non‑existent possible is receptive to existence; the first existence may increase preparedness, making the second existence easier. Finally, he interprets the verse 'and it is easier for Him' as referring to the re‑creation of disintegrated parts (not the re‑creation of something from absolute non‑existence), citing another verse about giving life to bones. The passage ends incompletely (cut off at page end).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that theological discussions about re‑creation are unrelated to actual religious beliefs and are just theoretical exercises.
Correction: Al‑Taftazani argues that such discussions are directly beneficial for proving the Resurrection and other eschatological doctrines, and for repelling doubts.

B) Because the author forgot to include it earlier

D) Because it was a later addition

Key Takeaway
Theological discussions like i'adat al‑ma'dum are not idle; they are essential for proving the Return, and different schools have nuanced positions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you encounter a complex theological debate like i'adat al-ma'dum, do you tend to dismiss it as irrelevant to your faith, or do you see it as a necessary shield for your certainty about the resurrection? What would change in your daily conviction about the Last Day if you understood the rational foundation more deeply?
?

Unseen Matters
advanced
7 min
The Detailed Proofs and the Qur'anic Argument
Al-Taftazani gives expanded proofs and interprets the verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fuller presentation of the iqna' and ilzam arguments, the discussion of increased preparedness, and how al‑Taftazani interprets the Qur'anic verse 'and it is easier for Him' to support the permissibility of re‑creation.

Core Idea
Re‑creation is easier for God because the first existence increases preparedness, not because re‑creation is a different kind of act.

Al‑Taftazani returns to the proofs with greater detail, reinforcing the principle of possibility and the identity of return and beginning. He then engages with the idea that the first existence confers a disposition (istidad) that makes the second existence easier, citing the Qur'an. He concludes with a clarification that the verse primarily refers to re‑assembling parts, not creating from absolute non‑existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the repeated but expanded iqna' argument
Al‑Taftazani repeats the iqna' argument but now adds the philosophers' maxim: 'Whatever strange thing reaches your ears, cast it into the realm of possibility as long as a clear proof does not prevent you.' Notice how this reinforces the burden of proof.
This maxim is attributed to the philosophers, showing common ground.
2
Examine the ilzam argument and its defense against an objection
The ilzam is restated with emphasis that times do not affect essential possibility. Then an objection is raised: that the return is a 'second existence', which is more specific than absolute existence. Al‑Taftazani refutes it by saying that the general possibility includes the specific.
The objection is about the logical relationship between general and specific.
3
Understand the concept of increased preparedness (istidad)
Some argue that the first existence gives the non‑existent a disposition (malaka) that makes the second existence easier. Al‑Taftazani finds this plausible and connects it to the verse 'and it is easier for Him'.
This is a natural explanation of why re‑creation might be 'easier'.
4
Interpret the Qur'anic verse correctly
Al‑Taftazani suggests that the easier re‑creation in the verse refers to the reconstitution of disintegrated parts (like resurrection of bodies), not to bringing something from absolute non‑existence. He cites another verse: 'Say: He will give them life who created them the first time.'
This shows a careful scriptural reasoning.

He said: The First Investigation – It is permissible to re-create the non-existent, contrary to the philosophers absolutely, and contrary to some Mu'tazila regarding accidents, and contrary to some of them regarding what does not remain among them, like sounds. For us, as an inducement (iqna'an), [we say] that the original principle is possibility (al-imkan) until a proof of necessity or impossibility arises, and as a compulsion (ilzaman) that the return (al-ma'ad) is like the beginning (al-mabda'), nay, it is the very same thing, so it is impossible for it to be possible at one time and impossible at another time. Rather, perhaps it is claimed that the first existence confers an increase of preparedness for receiving existence, as indicated by His saying, exalted is He, 'and it is easier for Him' (wa huwa ahwanu 'alayhi) – and there is a consideration in this. It cannot be said: perhaps it is impossible due to a necessary matter, for we say: either it is impossible or not. Many of the investigations of the theologians seem outwardly alien to the knowledge of religious beliefs, but when the original objectives are verified, it is known that they are beneficial in presenting proofs for them or repelling doubts about them. That includes re-creating the non‑existent, the subsistence of the atom, the void, the validity of annihilation for the world, the permissibility of breaking through the celestial spheres, the non‑conditionality of life by the body, the non‑necessity of the finitude of physical powers, and the like, in proving the Resurrection, the punishment of the grave, and eternal life in Paradise or Hell… and other things according to the diversity of opinions. He only delayed the discussion of re‑creating the non‑existent specifically to this place because of its special relevance to the matter of the Return (al‑ma'ad), since it is not needed except in proving the Return by way of existence after annihilation. The majority of theologians agreed on its permissibility, and the philosophers on its impossibility. As for the Mu'tazila, Basran sect held the permissibility of re‑creating substances, but based on the persistence of their essences in non‑existence, so that if they were annihilated, their re‑creation would be impossible. They differed regarding accidents. Some of them said: their re‑creation is absolutely impossible because the re‑created thing (al-mu'ad) is only re‑created by a meaning (bi-ma'nan), so it would entail the subsistence of a meaning by a meaning. Some of our companions (Ash'aris) held this view. They divided the persisting accidents into what is within the ability of the servant, and ruled that it is not permissible to re‑create them, neither for the servant nor for the Lord; and into what is not within the ability of the servant, and they permitted its re‑creation. For us, as an inducement (iqna'an), [we say] that the original principle regarding what there is no proof of its necessity or impossibility is possibility (al-imkan), as the philosophers said: 'Whatever strange thing reaches your ears, cast it into the realm of possibility as long as a clear proof does not prevent you.' So whoever claims the impossibility of re‑creating the non‑existent, the burden of proof is on him. And as a compulsion (ilzaman), that the return (al-ma'ad) is like the beginning (al-mabda'), nay, it is the very same thing, because the discussion is about re‑creating the very same non‑existent. It is impossible for a thing to be possible at one time and impossible at another, because it is certain that times have no effect on what is in itself [possible]. On this basis, what is said – that the return (al-'awd), which is existence a second time, is more specific than absolute existence, and the possibility of the more general does not entail the possibility of the more specific – is not valid. Close to this is what is said: the non‑existent possible is receptive to existence by the necessity of the impossibility of conversion; the first existence confers an increase of preparedness for receiving existence, as is the case of all recipients, based on acquiring a disposition for being qualified by the act. Commentary
This passage presents the first investigation (al-mabhas al-awwal) of the chapter on al-ma'ad, concerning the permissibility of re-creating the non-existent (i'adat al-ma'dum). The author begins by stating the position that it is permissible, contrary to the philosophers who deny it absolutely, and contrary to some Mu'tazila who restrict the denial to accidents or to non‑persistent accidents like sounds. He then gives two arguments: first, an inducement (iqna') that the default judgment for anything is possibility (al-imkan) until a proof establishes necessity or impossibility; second, a compulsion (ilzam) that the return (al-ma'ad) is like the beginning (al-mabda')—indeed the very same entity—so it cannot be possible at one time and impossible at another, since times have no effect on essential possibility. The author notes a possible claim that the first existence confers an increase of preparedness, citing the verse 'and it is easier for Him'. He then raises a consideration: an objection might be that the thing is impossible due to a necessary concomitant, but the response is that either that concomitant renders it impossible or not; if not, the objection fails. Following this, the author gives a methodological note explaining that many theological discussions appear unrelated to creed but are actually useful for defending doctrines. He delays the discussion of i'adat al-ma'dum to this chapter because it is specifically needed for proving the Resurrection as existence after annihilation. He then reports that the majority of theologians (mutakallimun) affirm permissibility, while philosophers (hukama') deny it. The Mu'tazila are divided: the Basran school allows re‑creation of substances based on persistence of essences in non‑existence, but differs on accidents. One group absolutely denies re‑creation of accidents on the ground that the re‑created thing would be re‑created by a meaning, leading to an infinite regress; some Ash'aris hold this. The majority deny re‑creation of non‑persistent accidents (sounds, wills) due to their time‑specificity, and for persistent accidents they distinguish between what is within human power (which cannot be re‑created by anyone) and what is not (which may be re‑created). The author then returns to his own school's proofs: the iqna' argument based on the principle of possibility (quoting the philosophers' maxim), and the ilzam argument that the return is identical to the beginning. He refutes the objection that 'return' (existence a second time) is more specific than absolute existence, so possibility of the general does not entail possibility of the specific. He also discusses a related point: the non‑existent possible is receptive to existence; the first existence may increase preparedness, making the second existence easier. Finally, he interprets the verse 'and it is easier for Him' as referring to the re‑creation of disintegrated parts (not the re‑creation of something from absolute non‑existence), citing another verse about giving life to bones. The passage ends incompletely (cut off at page end).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Qur'anic verse 'and it is easier for Him' means that re‑creation is inherently easier than initial creation, implying that both are possible but one is easier.
Correction: Al‑Taftazani explains that 'easier' does not imply a difference in possibility; it may refer to the re‑assembly of existing parts or the increased preparedness from the first existence.

Checkpoint
How does al‑Taftazani interpret the verse 'It is He who begins creation, then repeats it, and it is easier for Him' (30:27)?
A) As proof that re‑creating the non‑existent is absolutely possible
B) As referring to the re‑creation of disintegrated parts into their original forms
C) As indicating that God's power changes over time
D) As a metaphorical expression with no doctrinal weight

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic verse 'easier for Him' is best understood as referring to the reconstitution of bodies, not to creating from absolute non‑existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Do you truly believe that God can bring you back after death exactly as you are, with the same body and soul? How does that belief—or lack of it—shape the choices you make today?
?
The philosophers taught: 'Whatever strange thing reaches your ears, cast it into the realm of possibility as long as a clear proof does not prevent you.' What strange claim about the afterlife do you find hardest to accept, and have you honestly examined it in the light of revelation rather than dismissing it?
?
If re-creation is 'easier' for God, why do you still fear death and decay? What does your fear—or indifference—reveal about the depth of your trust in His power to restore you completely?

Objection Three: Identical Creation and the Problem of Distinction
The third objection and two refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection to the return of the non-existent exactly as it was, and how the refutations defend the possibility by appealing to real distinction beyond intellectual recognition.

Core Idea
Two identical things can be distinct in reality even if the intellect cannot distinguish them.

This passage presents the third objection against the view that the non-existent can be returned exactly as it was. The objection argues that if returning an identical is possible, then creating an identical originally would also be possible, leading to a lack of distinction. The refutations challenge the necessity of intellectual indistinguishability and show the objection is irrelevant to the issue at hand.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the whole passage to grasp the flow: objection, then two refutations. Note the logical structure: if returning is possible, then creating an identical originally should also be possible, which would lead to indistinguishability.
The objection relies on the principle that the judgment of similars is one.
2
Understand the problem of distinction
The objection claims that if two individuals share essence and all accidents, they cannot be distinguished, so they would not be two separate things. Identify why this is considered absurd by the objector.
3
Examine the first refutation
The first refutation denies that lack of intellectual distinction implies lack of real distinction. It argues that two things can be distinct in reality even if we cannot tell them apart intellectually.
The refutation points out that if they were not distinct, they would not be two things at all.
4
Examine the second refutation
The second refutation turns the objection on its head: if the proof were valid, it would also apply to the original creation of two similar individuals, which is not impossible. Thus the objection is irrelevant to the specific issue of returning the non-existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - لو جاز أن يعاد المعدوم بعينه لجاز أن يوجد ابتداء ما يماثله في الماهية وجميع العوارض المشخصة لأن حكم الأمثال واحد، ولأن التقدير أن وجود فرد بهذه الصفات من جملة الممكنات. واللازم باطل لعدم التميز بينه المعاد، لأن التقدير اشتراكهما في الماهية وجميع العوارض. ورد بأن عدم التميز في نفس الأمر غير لازم كيف ولو لم يتميزا لم يكونا شيئين، وعند العقل غير مسلم الاستحالة، إذ لا يلتبس، وعلى العقل ما هو متميز في نفس الأمر. وقد يجاب بأنه لو صح هذا الدليل لجاز وقوع شخصين متماثلين ابتداء بعين ما ذكرتم، ويلزم عدم التميز، وحاصله أنه لا تعلق لهذا بإعادة المعدوم.
The third: If it were permissible to return the non-existent exactly as it was, then it would be permissible for something similar to it in essence and all individuating accidents to exist originally, because the judgment of similars is one, and because it is assumed that the existence of an individual with these attributes is among the possibilities. The consequent is false due to the lack of distinction between it and the returned, because it is assumed that they share the essence and all accidents. It was replied that the lack of distinction in actual reality is not necessary; indeed, if they were not distinct they would not be two things, and in the intellect the impossibility is not conceded, since it is not confused, and what is distinct in actual reality is upon the intellect. And it may be answered that if this proof were valid, then it would be permissible for two similar individuals to occur originally in the same manner you mentioned, and lack of distinction would follow, and the gist is that this has no connection to the return of the non-existent.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection against the possibility of returning the non-existent exactly as it was, followed by two refutations. The objection argues that if it were possible to return an identical individual, then it would also be possible to create originally an individual identical in essence and all individuating accidents, because the rule for similars is the same. This would lead to a problem: there would be no distinction between the original and the returned individual, so they would not be two distinct things. The first refutation responds that lack of distinction in actual reality is not necessary; two things could still be two even if we cannot distinguish them, and the intellect does not deem such a situation impossible. The second refutation argues that if this proof were valid, it would also apply to the original creation of two similar individuals, leading to lack of distinction there too, thus the objection has nothing to do specifically with returning the non-existent.

Key Terms
إعادة المعدوم
تماثل
تميز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two identical individuals cannot be distinct in any sense, so their existence is impossible.
Correction: Two identical individuals can be distinct in actual reality even if the intellect cannot distinguish them. The refutation shows that intellectual indistinguishability does not entail real indistinguishability.

Checkpoint
What is the core premise of the third objection against returning the non-existent?
A) The returned must be exactly the same, which leads to infinite regress.
B) If returning an identical is possible, then creating an identical originally would also be possible, leading to lack of distinction.
C) The non-existent cannot be pointed to.
D) The judgment of similars is not one.

Key Takeaway
The third objection is refuted by showing that lack of intellectual distinction does not imply lack of real distinction, and that the objection applies to creation generally, not just to return.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you assumed that what you cannot distinguish is not real? How does this lesson challenge you to accept that Allah's knowledge includes distinctions beyond your perception? Identify one area of your life where you will trust Allah's knowledge over your own.
?
Do you treat people as indistinguishable because you don't see their unique worth? After learning that real distinction exists even when you cannot perceive it, what concrete change will you make to honor each person's individuality?
?
The refutation says lack of intellectual distinction does not entail lack of real distinction. Is there a matter of faith where your intellect fails to see a distinction (e.g., between divine mercy and justice, or between this world and the next)? How will you rely on revelation instead of your limited reasoning starting today?

The Fourth Objection: Cannot Point to the Non-Existent
Presents the fourth objection and Ash'ari refutation using mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the force of the objection that the non-existent cannot be pointed to and how the Ash'ari response uses intellectual existence to validate judgments about return.

Core Idea
Judgments about the non-existent need only mental existence, not physical pointing.

The fourth objection raises a physical impossibility: since the non-existent has no existence at all, it cannot be pointed to or referred to. This would make any judgment about its return impossible. The response, as articulated by Sanusi, argues that intellectual existence is sufficient for making rational judgments, drawing an analogy to ordinary statements about future possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage carefully to understand why pointing to the non-existent is considered impossible. The objection is that judgment requires a distinct subject with some existence.
Notice the key term 'إشارة' (pointing).
2
Identify the two answers
The passage mentions the Mu'tazili answer (since they hold the non-existent has existence) and the Ash'ari answer (intellectual existence is sufficient). Focus on the Ash'ari response.
3
Examine the refutation
The author refutes the claim that the judgment is merely mental by introducing a general concept: whatever the description applies to, the predicate applies. Understand this logical move.
The term 'قضية ذهنية' (mental proposition) is central.
4
Reflect on the analogy
The author ends with analogies: the possible non-existent may exist, a future child may learn. These show that mental judgments about external possibilities are valid.
Think of everyday examples where we judge about non-existent things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع - أن المعدوم تمتنع الإشارة إليه إذ لم يبق له ثبوت أصلاً، فيمتنع الحكم عليه بصحة العود، لأن الحكم بثبوت شيء لشيء يقتضي تميزه وثبوته في الجملة. والجواب عند المعتزلة القائلين بثبوت المعدوم وبقاء ذاته ظاهر. وعندنا أن التميز والثبوت عند العقل كاف في صحة الحكم والاحتياج إلى الثبوت العيني إنما هو عند ثبوت الصفة له في الخارج. وما يقال : إن القضية تكون حينئذ ذهنية . لا حقيقية، ولا خارجية، فلا يفيد إلا صحة العود في الذهن، ليس بشيء لأنا نأخذ للقضية مفهوماً عاماً هو أن ما يصدق عليه الوصف العنواني في الجملة يصدق عليه المحمول. فالمعنى ههنا أن ما يصدق عليه أنه معدوم في الخارج يصدق عليه أنه يوجد في الخارج. ولو سلم فالذهنية معناها أن الموضوع المأخوذ في الذهن محكوم عليه بالمحمول. فالمعنى ههنا أن المعنى الذهني المعدوم في الخارج يصح أن يعاد، ويوجد في الخارج. وبالجملة فهذا كما يقال : المعدوم الممكن يجوز أن يوجد، ومن سيولد يجوز أن يتعلم، إلى غير ذلك من الحكم على ما ليس بموجود في الخارج حال الحكم.
The fourth: The non-existent cannot be pointed to since it has no existence at all, so it is impossible to judge upon it as being valid for return, because judging that something is established for something requires its distinction and existence in some way. The answer according to the Mu'tazila who hold the existence of the non-existent and the permanence of its essence is apparent. According to us, distinction and existence in the intellect are sufficient for the validity of judgment, and the need for external existence is only when the attribute is established for it in the external. And what is said: that the proposition would then be mental, not real or external, so it only yields the validity of return in the mind, is nothing, because we take for the proposition a general concept: that whatever the descriptive attribute applies to in some way, the predicate applies to it. So the meaning here is that what is described as non-existent in the external is described as existing in the external. And if it is conceded, then the mental [proposition] means that the subject taken in the mind is judged by the predicate. So the meaning here is that the mental concept that is non-existent in the external is valid to be returned and to exist in the external. In short, this is like saying: the possible non-existent may exist, and he who will be born may learn, and other judgments upon what is not existent in the external at the time of judgment.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection from the impossibility of pointing to the non-existent. The objection states that since the non-existent has no existence at all, it cannot be referred to, and thus a judgment that it can return is impossible because judgment requires the subject to be distinct and have some existence. The answer from the Mu'tazila is straightforward since they posit existence for the non-existent. For the Ash'aris, the answer is that distinction and existence in the intellect are sufficient for making a judgment; external existence is only needed when the attribute is actually attached in the external world. The passage then refutes the claim that such a judgment is merely mental and not real, by explaining that the proposition's subject is a general concept, and the predicate applies to whatever the descriptive attribute applies to. Even if it is conceded as mental, the meaning is that the mental concept that is non-existent externally can be returned externally, analogous to saying a possible non-existent can exist or a future child can learn.

Key Terms
إشارة
ثبوت
قضية ذهنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A judgment about the non-existent in the mind cannot have any bearing on external reality.
Correction: The author explains that mental concepts can be used to make valid judgments about external possibilities, as when we say a future child may learn.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response, what is sufficient for making a judgment about the non-existent returning?
A) External existence of the non-existent.
B) Distinction and existence in the intellect.
C) Physical pointing.
D) The absence of all attributes.

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection fails because rational judgments about the non-existent only require intellectual existence, not physical pointing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you dismiss something as 'just in the mind' and therefore inconsequential? How might this lesson challenge you to take your intellectual convictions about the unseen more seriously?
?
The author uses the example 'he who will be born may learn' to show we judge future non-existent realities. Have you ever planned for your future based on mental existence? How does that affect your trust in Allah's decree?
?
If you truly believed that mental existence is a valid basis for judgment about eternal realities (like the return of creation), what would change in your daily actions and reliance on material evidence?

The Comprehensive Definition of I'ada
Defines i'ada and shows it resolves all objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the comprehensive definition of i'ada (return) and how it resolves objections against bodily resurrection.

Core Idea
Return means bringing back a thing with all its parts so that it is unmistakably recognized.

After presenting objections against resurrection, the author now provides a positive definition of i'ada that addresses all concerns. This passage clarifies what is meant by 'return' and shows that differences in time or debates about identity do not undermine the proof of resurrection. The analogy of repeating speech helps illustrate the concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the key terms and the flow of the argument.
The passage answers all objections collectively.
2
Examine the definition of i'ada
The author defines i'ada as bringing the thing into existence with all its parts and accidents, such that any observer is certain it is the same thing. Identify the components of this definition.
The definition includes both parts and accidents.
3
Analyze the analogy of speech
The author uses the analogy of repeating speech: when asked to repeat one's speech, the same letters with their composition and forms are intended, even though the second utterance is in a different time. Reflect on how this analogy clarifies resurrection.
Temporal difference does not negate identity in this sense.
4
Understand the sufficiency of the definition
The author asserts that this definition is sufficient to prove resurrection and is not invalidated by any of the previously raised objections. Consider why the quibble about 'same or similar' does not matter.
The focus is on recognizable identity, not metaphysical sameness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يجاب عن جميع الوجوه بأنا نعني بالإعادة أن يوجد ذلك الشيء الذي هو بجميع أجزائه وعوارضه، بحيث يقطع كل من يراه بأنه هو ذلك الشيء، كما يقال : أعد كلامك أي تلك الحروف بتأليفها وهيئاتها، ولا يضر كون هذا معاداً وفي زمان وذاك مبتدأ وفي زمان آخر ولا المناقشة أن هذا نفس الأول أو مثله. وهذا القدر كاف في إثبات الحشر ولا يبطل بشيء من الوجوه.
And it may be answered to all the aspects that we mean by return that that thing exists with all its parts and accidents, such that everyone who sees it is certain that it is that thing, just as it is said: repeat your speech, i.e., those letters with their composition and forms, and it does not harm that this is returned and in a time and that is originated and in another time, nor the quibbling that this is the first itself or similar to it. And this amount is sufficient to prove the resurrection and is not invalidated by any of the aspects.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive definition of 'return' (i'ada) that resolves all the preceding objections. The author defines return as bringing the thing into existence with all its parts and accidents, such that any observer is certain it is the same thing. He gives the analogy of repeating speech: a person says 'repeat your speech' meaning the same words with their composition and forms, even though the second utterance is in a different time. Temporal difference does not affect the identity in this sense. Similarly, quibbling whether the returned is the very first or just a similar does not invalidate the proof of resurrection. This definition is sufficient to establish the resurrection against all objections.

Key Terms
إعادة
أجزاء
عوارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that temporal difference or numerical identity debates undermine resurrection.
Correction: The definition of i'ada focuses on recognizable identity with all parts/accidents, unaffected by time.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to explain the meaning of 'return' (i'ada)?
A) Returning a borrowed item.
B) Repeating one's speech.
C) Rebuilding a house.
D) Reincarnation of a soul.

Key Takeaway
The comprehensive definition of i'ada as bringing back a thing with all its parts/accidents, illustrated by the analogy of repeating speech, suffices to prove resurrection against all objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Just as a speaker repeats his words and they are recognized as the same speech even in a different time, are you confident that your deeds in this time will be recognized by the same 'you' in the next? What are you doing to ensure that your 'you' in the hereafter is one you would want to meet?

Classification of Views on Resurrection
Sanusi classifies the main opinions on resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different positions on resurrection: denial, hesitation, affirmation with physical-only, spiritual-only, or both.

Core Idea
Views on resurrection range from denial to affirmation, with deep divides over its nature.

The author Sanusi presents a concise survey of opinions on the resurrection, showing that even among believers there is disagreement whether it is physical, spiritual, or both. This passage also notes the views of naturalists and Galen.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage listing opinions
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview of the different groups mentioned: naturalists, Galen, philosophers, and various Muslim groups.
Notice the order: denial, hesitation, then affirmation.
2
Identify the naturalist denial and Galen's hesitation
The naturalists deny resurrection because they consider man only the sensible body that perishes. Galen hesitated whether the soul is temperament or a permanent substance.
Galen's view is unique—he is not a denier but uncertain.
3
Note the division among affirmers
Affirmers are split: philosophers say resurrection is only spiritual; majority of Muslims say only physical; verifiers (like al-Ghazali) say both spiritual and physical. Also note the groups that follow each view.
This shows the richness of theological discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث الثاني - في اختلف الناس في المعاد فنفاه الطبيعيون ذهاباً إلى أن الإنسان هو هذا الهيكل المحسوس الذي يفنى بصورته وأعراضه فلا يعاد، وتوقف جالينوس لتردده في أن النفس هو المزاج أم جوهر باق، وأثبته الحكماء والمليون. إلا أنه عند الحكماء روحاني فقط وعند جمهور المسلمين جسماني فقط بناء على أن الروح جسم لطيف، وعند المحققين منهم كالغزالي، والحليمي، والراغب، والقاضي وأبي زيد روحاني وجسماني ذهاباً إلى تجرد النفس، وعليه أكثر الصوفية والشيعة والكرامية، وليس بتناسخ، لأنه عود في الدنيا إلى بدن ما، وهذا عود في الآخرة إلى بدن من الأجزاء الأصلية للبدن الأول، والقول بأنه ليس هو الأول بعينه لا يضر، وربما يؤيد بقوله تعالى: (كلما نضجت جلودهم بدلناهم جلوداً غيرها) وقوله تعالى: (أو ليس الذي خلق السموات والأرض بقادر على أن يخلق مثلهم بلی)
He said: The second section - concerning: People differed about the resurrection. The naturalists denied it, going to the view that man is this sensible structure which perishes with its form and accidents, so it is not returned. Galen hesitated due to his uncertainty whether the soul is the temperament or a permanent substance. The philosophers and the millat [religious communities] affirmed it. However, according to the philosophers it is only spiritual, and according to the majority of Muslims it is only physical based on that the soul is a subtle body. According to the verifiers among them like al-Ghazali, al-Halimi, al-Raghib, al-Qadi, and Abu Zaid, it is both spiritual and physical, going to the view of the soul's abstraction. And this is the view of most Sufis, Shi'a, and Karramiyya. And it is not reincarnation, because that would be a return in this world to some body, while this is a return in the Hereafter to a body from the original parts of the first body. And the statement that it is not the first itself does not harm. And it is perhaps supported by His statement: 'Whenever their skins are roasted, We replace them with other skins' and His statement: 'Or is not He who created the heavens and the earth able to create their like? Yes...'
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage classifies the different opinions on the resurrection. The naturalists deny it because they consider man to be only the sensible body that perishes completely. Galen hesitated. Philosophers and religious communities affirm it, but philosophers affirm only spiritual resurrection, while most Muslims affirm only physical resurrection based on the view that the soul is a subtle body. The verified scholars like al-Ghazali, al-Halimi, al-Raghib, al-Qadi, and Abu Zaid affirm both spiritual and physical resurrection, holding that the soul is abstract. This is also the view of most Sufis, Shi'a, and Karramiyya. The author distinguishes this from reincarnation (tanasukh) because reincarnation is a return in this world to some body, whereas resurrection is a return in the Hereafter to a body made from the original parts of the first body. The fact that the body may not be exactly the first in a numerical sense does not harm the doctrine, and Quranic verses support this: 'Whenever their skins are roasted, We replace them with other skins' and 'Or is not He who created the heavens and the earth able to create their like?'

Key Terms
المعاد
طبيعيون
جالينوس
حكماء
جسماني
روحاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Muslims agree that resurrection is only physical.
Correction: The passage shows that while the majority hold physical-only, verifiers and many Sufis, Shi'a, and Karramiyya affirm both spiritual and physical resurrection.

Checkpoint

A) Naturalists
B) Galen
C) Philosophers
D) Majority of Muslims

Key Takeaway
Opinions on resurrection vary widely: naturalists deny, Galen hesitates, philosophers affirm spiritual-only, majority of Muslims affirm physical-only, and verifiers affirm both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned about the verifiers who affirmed both spiritual and physical resurrection based on the soul's abstraction. Do you truly believe in a spiritual resurrection, or have you unconsciously reduced your hope to only physical re-creation? How does this impact how you live today?
?
The naturalists denied resurrection because they saw man as only a sensible structure. Where in your own life do you live as if this physical world is all there is, ignoring the eternal dimension? Name one specific habit that reveals such a mindset and commit to changing it.
?

Distinguishing Resurrection from Reincarnation
Sanusi distinguishes resurrection from reincarnation and cites Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author distinguishes bodily resurrection from reincarnation using scriptural evidence.

Core Idea
Resurrection is a return in the afterlife with original body parts, not a worldly rebirth.

After surveying opinions on resurrection, Sanusi clarifies a common confusion: how is resurrection different from the concept of reincarnation? He gives a clear distinction and supports it with Quranic verses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the distinction
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'وليس بتناسخ' and understand the two points of distinction: time (Hereafter vs. world) and body (original parts vs. any body).
Note that the author also addresses a potential objection about numerical identity.
2
Understand the key differences
Reincarnation (تناسخ) is a return in this world to some body, while resurrection is a return in the Hereafter to a body made from the original parts of the first body. This is the core distinction.
The phrase 'أجزاء أصلية' means 'original parts'.
3
Examine the Quranic support
The author cites two Quranic verses: one about changing skins (4:56) and one about creating their like (36:81). These support the possibility of recreating the body even if not identical in every detail.
The objection that the resurrected body is not numerically identical is dismissed as not harmful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث الثاني - في اختلف الناس في المعاد فنفاه الطبيعيون ذهاباً إلى أن الإنسان هو هذا الهيكل المحسوس الذي يفنى بصورته وأعراضه فلا يعاد، وتوقف جالينوس لتردده في أن النفس هو المزاج أم جوهر باق، وأثبته الحكماء والمليون. إلا أنه عند الحكماء روحاني فقط وعند جمهور المسلمين جسماني فقط بناء على أن الروح جسم لطيف، وعند المحققين منهم كالغزالي، والحليمي، والراغب، والقاضي وأبي زيد روحاني وجسماني ذهاباً إلى تجرد النفس، وعليه أكثر الصوفية والشيعة والكرامية، وليس بتناسخ، لأنه عود في الدنيا إلى بدن ما، وهذا عود في الآخرة إلى بدن من الأجزاء الأصلية للبدن الأول، والقول بأنه ليس هو الأول بعينه لا يضر، وربما يؤيد بقوله تعالى: (كلما نضجت جلودهم بدلناهم جلوداً غيرها) وقوله تعالى: (أو ليس الذي خلق السموات والأرض بقادر على أن يخلق مثلهم بلی)
He said: The second section - concerning: People differed about the resurrection. The naturalists denied it, going to the view that man is this sensible structure which perishes with its form and accidents, so it is not returned. Galen hesitated due to his uncertainty whether the soul is the temperament or a permanent substance. The philosophers and the millat [religious communities] affirmed it. However, according to the philosophers it is only spiritual, and according to the majority of Muslims it is only physical based on that the soul is a subtle body. According to the verifiers among them like al-Ghazali, al-Halimi, al-Raghib, al-Qadi, and Abu Zaid, it is both spiritual and physical, going to the view of the soul's abstraction. And this is the view of most Sufis, Shi'a, and Karramiyya. And it is not reincarnation, because that would be a return in this world to some body, while this is a return in the Hereafter to a body from the original parts of the first body. And the statement that it is not the first itself does not harm. And it is perhaps supported by His statement: 'Whenever their skins are roasted, We replace them with other skins' and His statement: 'Or is not He who created the heavens and the earth able to create their like? Yes...'
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage classifies the different opinions on the resurrection. The naturalists deny it because they consider man to be only the sensible body that perishes completely. Galen hesitated. Philosophers and religious communities affirm it, but philosophers affirm only spiritual resurrection, while most Muslims affirm only physical resurrection based on the view that the soul is a subtle body. The verified scholars like al-Ghazali, al-Halimi, al-Raghib, al-Qadi, and Abu Zaid affirm both spiritual and physical resurrection, holding that the soul is abstract. This is also the view of most Sufis, Shi'a, and Karramiyya. The author distinguishes this from reincarnation (tanasukh) because reincarnation is a return in this world to some body, whereas resurrection is a return in the Hereafter to a body made from the original parts of the first body. The fact that the body may not be exactly the first in a numerical sense does not harm the doctrine, and Quranic verses support this: 'Whenever their skins are roasted, We replace them with other skins' and 'Or is not He who created the heavens and the earth able to create their like?'

Key Terms
تناسخ
أجزاء أصلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Resurrection and reincarnation are essentially the same concept.
Correction: The passage clearly distinguishes them: reincarnation is a worldly return to any body, while resurrection is an otherworldly return to the original body.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a difference between resurrection and reincarnation?
A) Resurrection is spiritual only.
B) Reincarnation occurs in the Hereafter.
C) Resurrection occurs in the Hereafter with a body from the original parts.
D) Reincarnation is denied by all Muslims.

Key Takeaway
Resurrection is a unique Islamic doctrine distinct from reincarnation, grounded in the Quran.

Evidence for Bodily Resurrection from Revelation
Author proves bodily resurrection from hadith and Quran and rejects metaphorical interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses hadith and Quranic verses to prove the possibility and reality of bodily resurrection, and why interpreting them metaphorically is unacceptable.

Core Idea
Resurrection is proven by clear texts that cannot be metaphorical without accusing prophets of lying.

The author presents several hadith and Quranic verses that describe the physical details of the afterlife. He then argues that these texts cannot be interpreted as mere metaphors for spiritual resurrection, because doing so would undermine the truthfulness of the prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the specific hadith and verse citations.
The text mentions hairless people of Paradise and the size of a Hell-dweller's tooth.
2
Identify the author's main proof
The author quotes hadith and Quranic verses as direct evidence. What do they describe? Are they ambiguous?
The verses are about gathering bones and rushing from graves.
3
Understand the warning against metaphor
The author says interpreting these as metaphorical would accuse the prophets of lying. Why is this important?
Prophets are truthful by necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبما ورد في الحديث من كون أهل الجنة جرداً مرداً، وكون ضرس الجهنمي مثل أحد لنا أنه أمر ممكن أخبر به الصادق، إذ تواتر من نبينا القول به . وورد في التنزيل مالا يحتمل التأويل مثل : ( قُلْ يُحْيِيهَا الَّذِي أَنشَأَهَا أَوَّلَ مَرَّةٍ ) (۱) فَإِذَا هُم مِّنَ الْأَجْدَاثِ إِلَى رَبِّهِمْ يَنسِلُونَ (۲) وَقَوْلُهُ : أَيَحْسَبُ الْإِنسَانُ أَلَّن نَّجْمَعَ عِظَامَهُ (۳) يَوْمَ تَشَقَّقُ الْأَرْضُ عَنْهُمْ سِرَاعاً ذَلِكَ حَشْرٌ عَلَيْنَا يَسِيرٌ (٤) إلى غير ذلك من الآيات والأحاديث، وحملها على التمثيل للمعاد الروحاني ترغيباً وترهيباً للعوام، وتتميماً لأمر النظام نسبة للأنبياء إلى الكذب في التبليغ والقصد إلى التضليل . ) الفلاسفة الطبيعيون الذين لا يعتد بهم في المسألة، ولا في الفلسفة، أنه لا معاد للبشر اصلاً، زعماً منهم أنه هذا الهيكل المحسوس بما له من المزاج، والقوى، والأعراض ، وأن ذلك يفنى بالموت وزوال الحياة ، ولا يبقى إلا المواد العنصرية المتفرقة، وأنه لا إعادة للمعدوم، وفي هذا تكذيب للعقل على ما يراه المحققون من أهل الفلسفة، وللشرع على ما يراه المحققون من أهل الملة. وتوقف جالينوس في أمر المعاد لتردده في أن النفس هو المزاج، فيفنى بالموت، فلا يعاد أم جوهر باق بعد الموت يكون له المعاد.
And from what is reported in the hadith about the people of Paradise being hairless and beardless, and that the molar tooth of the person in Hell is like Mount Uhud, it is evidence for us that [the resurrection] is a possible matter which the truthful one informed about, since the statement about it is recurrently transmitted from our Prophet. And there came in the revelation what cannot bear metaphorical interpretation, such as: 'Say, He will give life to them who produced them the first time' (Ya-Sin 79) 'Then behold, from the graves they rush out to their Lord' (Ya-Sin 51) and His saying: 'Does man think that We will not assemble his bones?' (Al-Qiyamah 3) 'On the day when the earth is split open from them rapidly; that is a gathering easy for Us' (Qaf 44) and other verses and hadith. To interpret them as figurative representation for spiritual resurrection, as encouragement and threat for the common people, and to complete the order of the system, would be an attribution to the prophets of lying in conveying and intending to mislead. The naturalist philosophers, who are not counted in this matter nor in philosophy, [hold] that there is no resurrection for human beings at all, claiming that this sensible structure with its temperament, powers, and accidents perishes with death and the cessation of life, and only scattered elemental materials remain, and that there is no return for the non-existent. In this is a denial of reason, according to what the investigators among the philosophers hold, and of the Law, according to what the investigators among the people of the religion hold. And Galen hesitated about the matter of resurrection due to his indecision whether the soul is the temperament, and thus perishes with death and is not returned, or [whether it is] a substance remaining after death, to which resurrection applies.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a combined proof and refutation. The author begins by citing hadith and Quranic verses that explicitly describe physical details of resurrection (e.g., the appearance of Paradise's inhabitants, the size of a Hell-dweller's tooth, and verses about bones and graves). He argues that these texts cannot be interpreted metaphorically as referring only to a spiritual resurrection, because doing so would accuse the prophets of lying and intent to mislead the common people—which is impossible for prophets. He then refutes two groups: first, the naturalist philosophers who deny any resurrection entirely, claiming that only material elements remain and that the non-existent cannot be recreated. The author says that this position contradicts reason (as agreed by philosophic investigators) and religious law (as agreed by religious investigators). Second, he mentions Galen, who wavered on whether the soul is the temperament (which would perish) or a permanent substance. The author does not resolve Galen's doubt here but sets it up as an incomplete position.

Key Terms
المعاد
الروحاني
الجسماني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Resurrection verses can be interpreted purely metaphorically as spiritual resurrection.
Correction: The author argues that such interpretation would accuse prophets of lying and misleading, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
What does the author say would be the consequence of interpreting resurrection verses as metaphorical?
A) It would be acceptable for common people.
B) It would accuse the prophets of lying and misleading.
C) It would require additional proofs from reason.
D) It would only apply to spiritual resurrection.

Key Takeaway
Clear Quranic verses and hadith prove bodily resurrection, and interpreting them metaphorically would impugn prophetic truthfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Knowing that bodily resurrection is a certain reality, what one change in your daily life would reflect that you are truly waiting for that day when the earth splits open and you rush out to your Lord?

Refutation of Deniers of Resurrection: Naturalist Philosophers and Galen
Author refutes naturalist philosophers' denial of resurrection and notes Galen's hesitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of the naturalist philosophers who deny resurrection entirely and Galen's hesitation on the nature of the soul.

Core Idea
Denial of bodily resurrection contradicts both reason and revelation.

Having established the textual evidence, the author now turns to refute those who deny resurrection. He addresses two groups: the naturalist philosophers who claim resurrection is impossible, and Galen who was uncertain about the soul's nature.

Three Views on the Nature of Resurrection
Author classifies three positions on resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main theological positions on the nature of resurrection (ma'ad) and their key proponents.

Core Idea
Resurrection is agreed upon, but its modality divides theologians into three camps.

The author begins by noting that all investigators agree on the reality of resurrection, but differ on its modality. He then classifies the three main views: bodily only, spiritual only, and both. This passage introduces the key positions and their rationales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three views
Read the passage and list the three distinct positions on resurrection mentioned. Note which groups adhere to each view.
The views are: bodily only, spiritual only, and both together.
2
Understand the reasoning
For each view, pay attention to the specific reasoning provided. For example, why do the majority of Muslims hold bodily resurrection? Why do philosophers hold spiritual only?
The reasoning is tied to the nature of the soul and body.
3
Note the proponents
Observe that the third view (both) is held not only by many Islamic scholars but also by Christians and reincarnationists. This is a key point against thinking the view is uniquely Islamic.
The author lists specific names and groups.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واتفق المحققون من الفلاسفة والمليين على حقية المعاد واختلفوا في كيفيته ، فذهب جمهور المسلمين إلى أنه جسماني فقط ، لأن الروح عندهم جسم سار في البدن سريان النار في الفحم، والماء في الورد . وذهب الفلاسفة إلى أنه روحاني فقط ، لأن البدن ينعدم بصوره وأعراضه فلا يعاد . والنفس جوهر مجرد باق لا سبيل إليه للفناء، فيعود إلى عالم المجردات بقطع التعلقات . وذهب كثير من علماء الإسلام كالإمام الغزالي ) والكعبي، والحليمي، والراغب (۳) ، والقاضي أبي زيد الدبوسي إلى القول بالمعاد الروحاني والجسماني جميعاً، ذهاباً إلى أن النفس جوهر مجرد يعود إلى البدن . وهذا رأي كثير من الصوفية، والشيعة والكرامية، وبه يقول جمهور النصارى، والتناسخية.
And the investigators among the philosophers and the people of the religion have agreed on the reality of the resurrection, but they differed concerning its modality. The majority of Muslims held that it is only bodily, because the spirit according to them is a body flowing in the body like the flow of fire in charcoal and water in a rose. The philosophers held that it is only spiritual, because the body becomes non-existent with its forms and accidents, so it is not returned; and the soul is a self-subsistent substance that has no way to annihilation, so it returns to the world of self-subsistent beings by cutting off attachments. And many scholars of Islam, such as al-Imam al-Ghazali, al-Ka'bi, al-Halimi, al-Raghib, and al-Qadi Abu Zayd al-Dabusi, held the position of both spiritual and bodily resurrection together, going to the view that the soul is a self-subsistent substance that returns to the body. This is the opinion of many Sufis, Shi'is, and Karramiyya, and the majority of Christians and reincarnationists hold it.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage classifies the three main views on the nature of resurrection (ma'ad). The author first notes that all investigators agree on the fact of resurrection but differ on its modality. He then enumerates the three positions: 1) The majority of Muslims hold that resurrection is only bodily (jismani faqat) because they consider the spirit (ruh) to be a body permeating the physical body, similar to fire in charcoal or water in a rose. 2) The philosophers hold that resurrection is only spiritual (ruhani faqat) because the body perishes entirely with its forms and accidents and cannot be restored, while the soul is a self-subsistent substance (jawhar mujarrad) that remains and returns to the world of self-subsistent beings. 3) Many Islamic scholars—including al-Ghazali, al-Ka'bi, al-Halimi, al-Raghib, and al-Dabusi—hold that both spiritual and bodily resurrection occur together, because the soul is a self-subsistent substance that returns to the body. The author adds that this third view is also held by many Sufis, Shi'is, and Karramiyya, as well as the majority of Christians and reincarnationists.

Key Terms
المعاد
جسماني
روحاني
النفس
جوهر مجرد
الروح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The view that resurrection is both spiritual and bodily is unique to Islam.
Correction: The author shows it is also held by Christians and reincarnationists, though with different theological frameworks.

Checkpoint

A) The soul is a self-subsistent substance that returns to the body.
B) The spirit is a body that flows in the physical body like fire in charcoal.
C) The body does not perish and remains after death.
D) The philosophers forced them to adopt this view.

Key Takeaway
The nature of resurrection is disputed among theologians, with three main positions: bodily only, spiritual only, and both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you more attached to the body or to the soul? What concrete step can you take this week to strengthen your soul's connection to its Creator?

Are you willing to accept a belief even if it is shared by others you consider misguided? What does that say about the state of your heart?

Distinction from Reincarnationists on the Return of Spirits
Al-Razi contrasts Muslim and reincarnationist views on spirits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key differences between the Muslim and reincarnationist views on the origin and return of spirits, and why the Muslim view is not heretical.

Core Idea
Muslims affirm created spirits returning in the afterlife; reincarnationists affirm pre-eternal spirits returning in this world.

Imam al-Razi clarifies a crucial distinction that prevents confusion between Islamic resurrection doctrine and reincarnationist beliefs. Common people might mistakenly equate the two, but the differences are fundamental.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how al-Razi contrasts the two positions.
The passage begins with 'قال الإمام الرازي' and ends at 'كذا في نهاية العقول'.
2
Identify the two contrasting views
List the Muslim position and the reincarnationist position. What are the differences in the origin of spirits and the timing of their return?
Muslims: hadith al-arwah, return in akhira. Reincarnationists: qidam al-arwah, return in this world.
3
Understand the common misconception
Note why common people think the Muslim view is kufr. Al-Razi explains that it's not the view itself but the additional denials of reincarnationists and Christians that cause unbelief.
Reincarnationists deny the Hereafter; Christians affirm Trinity. Muslims affirm both bodily and spiritual resurrection.
4
Learn the purpose of this distinction
Al-Razi quotes the Nihayat al-'Uqul to show that affirming self-subsistent souls (al-nufus al-mujarrada) is not a root of religion but can support the proof of resurrection.
This shows that the doctrine of spiritual resurrection is not inherently problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام الرازي: إلا أن الفرق أن المسلمين يقولون بحدوث الأرواح وردها إلى الأبدان لا في هذا العالم ، بل في الآخرة، والتناسخية بقدمها وردها إليها في هذا العالم وينكرون الآخرة والجنة والنار وإنما نبهنا على هذا الفرق لأنه يغلب على الطباع العامية أن هذا المذهب يجب ان يكون كفراً وضلالاً، لكونه مما ذهب إليه التناسخية والنصارى، ولا يعلمون أن التناسخية إنما يكفرون لإنكارهم القيامة والجنة والنار، والنصارى لقولهم بالتثليث . وأما القول بالنفوس المجردة فلا اصلا من أصول الدين بل ربما يؤيده ويبين الطريق إلى إثبات المعاد بحيث لا يقدح فيه شبه المنكرين ، كذا في نهاية العقول . وقد بالغ الإمام الغزالي في تحقيق المعاد الروحاني، وبيان أنواع الثواب والعقاب بالنسبة إلى الروح ، حتى سبق إلى كثير من الأوهام، ووقع في ألسنة بعض العوام أنه ينكر حشر الأجساد افتراء عليه . كيف وقد صرح به في مواضع من كتاب الإحياء وغيره، وذهب إلى أن إنكاره كفر. وإنما لم يشرحه في كتبه كثير شرح ، لما قال أنه ظاهر لا يحتاج إلى زيادة بيان . نعم ربما يميل كلامه وكلام كثير من القائلين بالمعادين إلى أن معنی ذلك أن يخلق الله تعالى من الأجزاء المتفرقة لذلك البدن بدناً، فيعيد إليه نفسه المجردة الباقية بعد خراب البدن ولا يضرنا كونه غير البدن الأول، بحسب الشخص، ولامتناع (۱) هو محمد بن محمد بن محمد الغزالي الطوسي ، أبو حامد حجة الإسلام، فيلسوف متصوف له نحو مئتي مصنف ولد عام ٤٥٠ هـ ووفاته عام ٥٠٥هـ رحل إلى نيسابور ثم إلى بغداد فالحجاز فيلاد الشام فمصر وعاد إلى بلدته من كتبه إحياء علوم الدين وتهافت الفلاسفة، وغير ذلك كثير . (۲) هو الحسين بن محمد بن المفضل أبو القاسم الأصفهاني المعروف بالراغب، أديب من الحكماء العلماء من أهل أصبهان سكن بغداد واشتهر حتى كان يقرن بالإمام الغزالي من كتبه محاضرات الأدباء، والذريعة إلى مكارم الشريعة، والأخلاق، ويسمى أخلاق الراغب وجامع التفاسير، والمفردات في غريب القرآن وغير ذلك كثير توفي عام ٥٠٢ هـ راجع روضات الجنات ٢٤٩ وتاريخ حكماء الإسلام ۱۱۲.
al-Imam al-Razi said: However, the difference is that Muslims affirm the temporal origination of spirits and their return to bodies, not in this world, but in the Hereafter, while the reincarnationists affirm their pre-eternity and their return to them in this world, and they deny the Hereafter, Paradise, and Hell. We only drew attention to this difference because it often prevails upon common minds that this doctrine must be unbelief and error, because it is held by reincarnationists and Christians, and they do not know that the reincarnationists are only deemed unbelievers for their denial of the Resurrection, Paradise, and Hell, and the Christians for their assertion of the Trinity. As for the assertion of self-subsistent souls, it is not a root of the religion at all; rather, it may perhaps support it and clarify the way to establishing the resurrection such that no suspicion of the deniers can impeach it, as stated in Nihayat al-'Uqul. And al-Imam al-Ghazali went to great lengths in investigating the spiritual resurrection and explaining the types of reward and punishment in relation to the spirit, to the extent that it occurred to many minds and fell upon the tongues of some common people that he denies the assembling of bodies - a slander against him. How could that be, when he explicitly stated it in places in the Ihya' and other books, and held that denying it is unbelief. He only did not elaborate on it much in his books because he said it is so apparent that it needs no further explanation. Yes, perhaps his words and the words of many who affirm both resurrections tend to mean that God Most High creates from the scattered parts of that body a body, and returns to it its self-subsistent soul remaining after the ruin of the body, and it does not harm us that it is not the first body in terms of individual identity, and to prevent... (1) He is Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-Ghazali al-Tusi, Abu Hamid, Proof of Islam, a philosopher-mystic who authored about two hundred works, born in 450 AH, died in 505 AH; he traveled to Nishapur, then to Baghdad, then to Hijaz, Syria, and Egypt, and returned to his hometown. Among his books are Ihya' 'Ulum al-Din and Tahafut al-Falasifah, and many others. (2) He is al-Husayn ibn Muhammad ibn al-Mufaddal Abu al-Qasim al-Isfahani, known as al-Raghib, a literary man among the philosophers and scholars from Isfahan; he lived in Baghdad and became famous until he was compared to al-Imam al-Ghazali. Among his books are Muhadarat al-Udaba', al-Dhari'ah ila Makarim al-Shari'ah, and al-Akhlaq, also called Akhlaq al-Raghib, and Jami' al-Tafasir, and al-Mufradat fi Gharib al-Qur'an, and many others; he died in 502 AH. See Rawdat al-Jannat 249 and Tarikh Hukama' al-Islam 112.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage serves to clarify a key distinction and to defend al-Ghazali. The author quotes al-Imam al-Razi to explain the difference between the Muslim position (which affirms both bodily and spiritual resurrection) and the reincarnationist position: Muslims hold that spirits are created (hadith) and return to bodies only in the afterlife, while reincarnationists hold spirits are pre-eternal (qadim) and return to bodies in this world, denying the Hereafter. The author notes that common people might mistakenly think the Muslim view of spiritual resurrection is tantamount to unbelief because it resembles the views of reincarnationists and Christians, but he clarifies that reincarnationists are condemned for denying the Hereafter, and Christians for the Trinity—not for affirming spiritual resurrection. The author then defends al-Ghazali against the accusation that he denied bodily resurrection. He points out that al-Ghazali explicitly affirmed it in his works and considered denial of it as unbelief. The reason he did not elaborate much on bodily resurrection in his writings is that he considered it self-evident. Finally, the author suggests that al-Ghazali's view—and that of others who affirm both resurrections—may be that God creates a new body from the scattered parts of the original body and returns the same self-subsistent soul to it; numerical identity of the body is not required. The passage ends with a footnote providing biographical details of al-Ghazali and al-Raghib.

Key Terms
حدوث الأرواح
قدم الأرواح
التناسخ
النفوس المجردة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Muslim doctrine of spiritual resurrection is heretical because it resembles reincarnationist or Christian beliefs.
Correction: The heresy of reincarnationists stems from denying the afterlife, not from affirming the return of spirits. Christians are heretical for the Trinity, not for spiritual resurrection. The Islamic view affirms both bodily and spiritual resurrection in the afterlife.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, why are reincarnationists considered unbelievers?
A) Because they affirm the pre-eternity of spirits.
B) Because they deny the Resurrection, Paradise, and Hell.
C) Because they claim spirits return to bodies.
D) Because they reject the concept of self-subsistent souls.

Key Takeaway
The Islamic doctrine of spiritual resurrection is distinct from reincarnationist belief; the latter is heretical because of denying the afterlife, not because of affirming the return of spirits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a true belief because it outwardly resembled a false one from another religion or sect? Al-Razi warns against this trap. Where in your own heart have you taken shortcuts by judging by appearance rather than substance? Will you ask Allah to purify your intention to seek truth regardless of who else holds it?
?
The concept of self-subsistent souls is not required by faith, but al-Razi says it can be used to strengthen the proof of resurrection. Is there an idea or tool from philosophy or science that you have avoided because you feared it might be tainted? What would it cost you to consider it humbly, not as doctrine, but as a means to deepen your conviction?
?
Al-Razi distinguishes between heretics who deny the afterlife and those who affirm it but have a particular view on souls. How often do you conflate people's errors, assuming that if a group is wrong on one point, they must be wrong on another? Can you, like al-Razi, learn to separate issues and judge each claim on its own merits, even when dealing with those you disagree with?

Defense of al-Ghazali on Bodily Resurrection
Defends al-Ghazali and explains his view on bodily resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ghazali affirmed bodily resurrection and why accusations of denial are false, along with his nuanced explanation of the resurrected body.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali affirmed bodily resurrection; the accusation is a slander.

The passage defends al-Ghazali from the charge that he denied the bodily resurrection (harsh al-ajsad). It explains that al-Ghazali considered it self-evident and elaborated mainly on spiritual resurrection, but explicitly affirmed bodily resurrection in his works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the accusation and al-Ghazali's actual stance.
The passage starts with 'وقد بالغ الإمام الغزالي' and ends with 'ولامتناع'.
2
Identify the accusation and its refutation
Find where the author states the false accusation against al-Ghazali and how he refutes it by citing al-Ghazali's own words.
The accusation: he denies harshr al-ajsad. Refutation: he explicitly stated it in Ihya' and other books, and considered denial as kufr.
3
Understand al-Ghazali's reason for not elaborating
Al-Ghazali thought bodily resurrection is so apparent that it needs no detailed explanation, which is why he focused on spiritual resurrection.
This explains why some might misunderstand his emphasis.
4
Analyze the identity of the resurrected body
The author presents a possible interpretation: God creates a new body from the scattered parts and returns the same soul. Numerical identity is not required.
This clarifies the philosophical nuance without contradicting orthodoxy.
