First Objection: Recreating What Has Perished
Al-Taftazani presents the first objection of the deniers and its brief rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against bodily resurrection and the rebuttal that denies its two premises.

Core Idea
The first objection fails because its premises are simply denied.

Al-Taftazani begins the discussion of objections against bodily resurrection by presenting the first argument of the deniers. They claim that resurrection requires recreating what has completely perished, which they deem impossible. The rebuttal is swift: both premises of the objection are rejected outright. This lesson unpacks that objection and its counter.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: the objection (two premises) and the rebuttal.
The passage is short but dense; each word matters.
2
Identify the two premises
The objection has two premises: (1) that the body's mixture, life, composition, and forms have perished, and (2) that recreating them is impossible. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the rebuttal
Al-Taftazani says the objection is 'replied' by denying both premises. Reflect on what that means for the argument.
The denial is not elaborated here; it is a placeholder for later refutation.
4
Check your understanding
Take the checkpoint quiz to confirm you know the core point of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : احتج المنكرون بوجوه : الأول - (الأول) - انه مبني على إعادة المعدوم للقطع بفناء المزاج والحياة والتأليف والهيئات وقد ثبت استحالتها ورد بمنع المقدمتين .
He said: The deniers argued with several points: The first: That it (bodily resurrection) is based on recreating what has perished (i'ādat al-ma'dūm), because it is certain that the mixture (mizāj), life, composition (ta'līf), and forms (hay'āt) have perished, and their impossibility has been established. It was replied by denying the two premises.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection of those who deny bodily resurrection. The objection states that bodily resurrection is based on the concept of recreating what has ceased to exist (i'ādat al-ma'dūm). They argue that it is certain that the specific mixture (mizāj), life, composition (ta'līf), and forms (hay'āt) of the body have perished at death, and their impossibility of being recreated has been established. The rebuttal is very brief: the two premises (that these have perished and that it is impossible to recreate them) are simply denied. The author does not elaborate here, leaving the detailed response to later discussions.

Key Terms
إعادة المعدوم
المزاج
التأليف
الهيئات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bodily resurrection requires recreating exactly the same material atoms that composed the body before death.
Correction: The objection assumes this, but the rebuttal denies that the original matter has perished completely or that recreation is impossible; later Islamic theology explains that Allah can recreate from a remnant (e.g., the coccyx bone) or create anew.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection against bodily resurrection mentioned in the text?
A) It requires the return of the soul to a new body
B) It requires recreating what has perished, which is impossible
C) It is not mentioned in the Quran
D) It contradicts reason

Key Takeaway
The first objection to bodily resurrection is based on the alleged impossibility of recreating what has perished, but it is dismissed by denying its two premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah can recreate you after death, even if your body has completely decayed? If yes, does that belief affect how you live today? What specifically will you do differently this week to prepare for that day?
?
The deniers argued from what they considered certain. Are there any 'certainties' in your life (like the impossibility of change, or the finality of death) that you need to re-examine in light of Allah's power? How would you practically remind yourself of this?
?
The rebuttal denies the premises. Are there doubts about the afterlife that you have accepted without questioning? What would it take for you to fully embrace the truth of bodily resurrection?

The Cannibalism Objection and the Reply of Original Parts
Al-Taftazani presents the cannibalism objection and the reply of original parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the cannibalism objection to bodily resurrection and how Al-Taftazani replies by distinguishing original parts from consumed parts.

Core Idea
Only the original parts of the body are resurrected, not the consumed parts.

One of the most famous objections to the resurrection of the body is the cannibalism problem: if a person eats another, how can both individuals be resurrected with their own bodies? Al-Taftazani presents this objection and then gives the Ash'ari response, which centers on the concept of 'original parts' (al-ajzā' al-aṣliyya). This lesson walks through the objection and its reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Notice the structure: an objection presented, then a reply.
The passage begins with 'الثاني' indicating this is the second objection.
2
Identify the three scenarios of the objection
The objection considers where eaten parts go: to the eater's body, to the eaten's body, or both. Each leads to a problem.
The text says 'إما أن تعاد في بدن الأكل... أو بالعكس... ولا سبيل إلى جعلها جزءاً من كل منهما'.
3
Understand the ethical problem
The objection raises an injustice: if a believer is eaten by a disbeliever, the obedient parts would be punished.
This is mentioned in 'تنعيم الأجزاء العاصية، وتعذيب المطيعة'.
4
Grasp the reply: original parts
The reply states that what is returned are the original parts, not the consumed parts. God preserves them from becoming original to another body.
Key phrase: 'ورد بأن المعاد هي الأجزاء الأصلية'.
5
Note the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila go further, saying this preservation is necessary for justice.
They say 'يجب ذلك ليصل الجزاء إلى مستحقه'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - لو أكل إنسان إنساناً فالأجزاء المأكولة إما أن تعاد في بدن الأكل، فلا يكون المأكول بعينه معاداً أو بالعكس، على أن لا أولوية، ولا سبيل إلى جعلها جزءاً من كل منهما، وأنه يلزم في أكل الكافر المؤمن تنعيم الأجزاء العاصية، وتعذيب المطيعة، ورد بأن المعاد هي الأجزاء الأصلية، فلا محذور، ولعل الله يحفظها من أن تصير جزءاً أصلياً لبدن آخر . بل عند المعتزلة يجب ذلك ليصل الجزاء إلى مستحقه . فإن قيل: مثل من يحيي العظام وهي رميم أئذا متنا وكنا تراباً يشعر بأن المتنازع إعادة الأجزاء بأسرها. قلنا لأنه ورد إزالة لاستبعادهم إحياء الرميم والتراب، والوارد لإثبات نفس الإعادة ايضاً كثير مثل : وهو الذي يبدأ الخلق ثم يعيده (۱) فسيقولون من يعيدنا قل الذي فطركم أول مرة ) ) (۲)
The second: If one human eats another human, then the eaten parts are either returned in the body of the eater, so the eaten person is not returned as the same individual, or the opposite, without any priority, and there is no way to make them a part of both; and it would necessitate, in the case of a believer eaten by a disbeliever, the reward of the disobedient parts and the punishment of the obedient parts. It was replied that what is returned are the original parts (al-ajzā' al-aṣliyya), so there is no difficulty, and perhaps God preserves them from becoming original parts of another body. In fact, according to the Mu'tazila, that (preservation) is necessary so that retribution reaches its due. If it is said: The example of 'Who revives bones while they are decayed' and 'When we have died and become dust' indicates that the matter in dispute is the return of all parts. We say: It came to remove their astonishment at the reviving of decayed bones and dust, and the verses that establish the very return are also many, such as 'And He it is who begins creation, then repeats it' and 'They will say: Who will return us? Say: He who first brought you into existence.'
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection of the deniers, known as the cannibalism problem. If a person eats another, how can the consumed parts be returned to both individuals? Either they go to the eater's body, then the eaten is not fully returned, or to the eaten's body, then the eater's body is incomplete. There is no way to assign them to both. Also, if a believer is eaten by a disbeliever, the obedient parts would be punished in the disbeliever's body and the disobedient parts rewarded in the believer's body, which is unjust. The answer: what is resurrected are the original parts (al-ajzā' al-aṣliyya) that constitute the individual's identity, not the consumed parts. God preserves those original parts from being incorporated as original parts of another body. The Mu'tazila even consider this preservation necessary for justice. A further objection is raised citing verses like 'Who revives bones while they are decayed?' which seems to imply the restoration of all parts. The reply: these verses are meant to remove their disbelief in the possibility of reviving decayed matter; they do not imply that every atom is restored. There are other explicit verses, such as 'He begins creation, then repeats it' and 'Who will return us? Say: He who first brought you into existence,' which clearly establish the return of the same individual.

Key Terms
الأجزاء الأصلية
إعادة
تنعيم
تعذيب
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that every atom of the body must return exactly as it was for resurrection to be valid.
Correction: The text clarifies that only the original parts (al-ajzā' al-aṣliyya) are returned; consumed parts do not need to be restored.

Checkpoint
According to the reply to the cannibalism objection, what parts of the body are resurrected?
A) All parts
B) The parts that were eaten
C) The original parts only
D) The soul only

Key Takeaway
The resurrection of the body requires only the return of the original parts that constitute personal identity, not all consumed parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If only your 'original parts' will be restored, what does that mean for the way you treat your body today? Are you living as if your physical actions will be preserved and brought back?
?
The passage says that God preserves the original parts even if they are consumed by another. Does this truth change how you trust God's justice when you see evil seemingly unchallenged? How should it change your response to injustice?
?
Consider that the Mu'tazila argued preservation is necessary for justice. Do you live with the certainty that every part of your life—every deed and every thought—will be brought back for judgment? What is one habit you need to change because of this certainty?

The Scriptural Objection and the Distinction Between Possibility and Actuality
Al-Taftazani rebuts an objection from Quranic verses and provides supporting verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Taftazani responds to objections from Quranic verses that seem to imply the return of all parts.

Core Idea
Quranic verses about reviving bones prove possibility, not that every atom returns.

Some object that Quranic verses like 'Who revives bones while they are decayed' imply the return of all parts. Al-Taftazani replies that such verses are meant to show God's power over decayed matter, not to describe the exact mechanism. He then cites other verses that explicitly affirm the return of the same individual. This lesson examines this exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the objection and the rebuttal.
The passage begins with 'فإن قيل' indicating an objection.
2
Identify the scriptural objection
The objector cites verses like 'Who revives bones while they are decayed' to argue that all parts must return.
The phrase 'يشعر بأن المتنازع إعادة الأجزاء بأسرها' shows the objector's conclusion.
3
Understand the reply: purpose of those verses
Al-Taftazani says those verses were revealed to remove astonishment at God's power, not to claim that every atom returns.
Key: 'قلنا لأنه ورد إزالة لاستبعادهم إحياء الرميم والتراب'.
4
Note the supporting verses
He then cites other verses that explicitly affirm the return itself, like 'He begins creation then repeats it'.
These verses directly support the doctrine of resurrection without specifying atom-by-atom return.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - لو أكل إنسان إنساناً فالأجزاء المأكولة إما أن تعاد في بدن الأكل، فلا يكون المأكول بعينه معاداً أو بالعكس، على أن لا أولوية، ولا سبيل إلى جعلها جزءاً من كل منهما، وأنه يلزم في أكل الكافر المؤمن تنعيم الأجزاء العاصية، وتعذيب المطيعة، ورد بأن المعاد هي الأجزاء الأصلية، فلا محذور، ولعل الله يحفظها من أن تصير جزءاً أصلياً لبدن آخر . بل عند المعتزلة يجب ذلك ليصل الجزاء إلى مستحقه . فإن قيل: مثل من يحيي العظام وهي رميم أئذا متنا وكنا تراباً يشعر بأن المتنازع إعادة الأجزاء بأسرها. قلنا لأنه ورد إزالة لاستبعادهم إحياء الرميم والتراب، والوارد لإثبات نفس الإعادة ايضاً كثير مثل : وهو الذي يبدأ الخلق ثم يعيده (۱) فسيقولون من يعيدنا قل الذي فطركم أول مرة ) ) (۲)
The second: If one human eats another human, then the eaten parts are either returned in the body of the eater, so the eaten person is not returned as the same individual, or the opposite, without any priority, and there is no way to make them a part of both; and it would necessitate, in the case of a believer eaten by a disbeliever, the reward of the disobedient parts and the punishment of the obedient parts. It was replied that what is returned are the original parts (al-ajzā' al-aṣliyya), so there is no difficulty, and perhaps God preserves them from becoming original parts of another body. In fact, according to the Mu'tazila, that (preservation) is necessary so that retribution reaches its due. If it is said: The example of 'Who revives bones while they are decayed' and 'When we have died and become dust' indicates that the matter in dispute is the return of all parts. We say: It came to remove their astonishment at the reviving of decayed bones and dust, and the verses that establish the very return are also many, such as 'And He it is who begins creation, then repeats it' and 'They will say: Who will return us? Say: He who first brought you into existence.'

This passage presents the second objection of the deniers, known as the cannibalism problem. If a person eats another, how can the consumed parts be returned to both individuals? Either they go to the eater's body, then the eaten is not fully returned, or to the eaten's body, then the eater's body is incomplete. There is no way to assign them to both. Also, if a believer is eaten by a disbeliever, the obedient parts would be punished in the disbeliever's body and the disobedient parts rewarded in the believer's body, which is unjust. According to Al-Taftazani, what is the purpose of verses like 'Who revives bones while they are decayed'?

?

Third Objection on Resurrection: Purpose and Refutation
Al-Taftazani refutes the claim that resurrection must have a purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against bodily resurrection concerning purpose and al-Taftazani's threefold refutation.

Core Idea
Resurrection does not require a purpose in the way critics assume.

The third objection argues that resurrection must have a purpose, but possible purposes are flawed or imply deficiency. Al-Taftazani refutes this by denying the necessity of purpose, expanding the range of acceptable purposes, and rejecting the reduction of afterlife pleasure to mere relief from pain. This lesson unpacks the objection and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the trilemma of purpose
Read the first part of the passage that presents the three possibilities for resurrection: without purpose (futile), for God's benefit (deficiency), or for the servant's benefit (pain or pleasure).
Notice how the objector tries to force a flawed set of options.
2
Examine the three denials
Identify al-Taftazani's three refutations: denying that resurrection must have a purpose, denying that the mentioned purposes are the only possibilities, and denying that afterlife pleasure is merely removal of pain.
Each denial targets a different assumption in the objection.
3
Grasp the counter-proposal of retribution
Focus on the idea that delivering retribution to the one who deserves it is a valid purpose, independent of the objector's flawed alternatives.
This shows that the objection fails to consider all possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - أن الإعادة لا لغرض عبث ، ولغرض عائد إلى الله تعالى نقص، وإلى العبد إما إيصال ألم، وهو سفه، أو لذة، ولا لذة في الوجود، سيما في عالم الحس إذ هي خلاص عن ألم والإيلام ليعقبه الخلاص غير لائق بالحكمة . بمنع لزوم الغرض، ومنع انحصاره فيما ذكر، إذ ربما يكون إيصال الجزاء إلى المستحق غرضاً، ومنع كون اللذة الأخروية دفع الألم) .
The third: That the return (al-i'āda) is either without purpose, which is futile ('abath), or for a purpose returning to God, which is a deficiency (naqṣ), or for a purpose returning to the servant, which is either to cause pain, which is foolishness (safah), or pleasure, and there is no pleasure in existence, especially in the sensory world, since it is only relief from pain; and causing pain to be followed by relief is not fitting for wisdom. [It was replied] by denying the necessity of purpose, and denying its restriction to what was mentioned, for perhaps delivering retribution to the one who deserves it is a purpose, and denying that the afterlife pleasure is merely the removal of pain.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection against bodily resurrection, concerning purpose. The objector argues that resurrection must have a purpose. If it has no purpose, it is futile (عبث). If the purpose returns to God, that implies a deficiency in God. If the purpose returns to the servant, it must be either to cause pain (which is foolish) or to give pleasure. But pleasure in the sensory world is only relief from pain, and causing pain only to later relieve it is not wise. The answer denies three things: first, the necessity that resurrection must have a purpose; second, the restriction of possible purposes to those mentioned, because delivering retribution to the deserving is a valid purpose; third, the claim that afterlife pleasure is merely the removal of pain. Thus, the objection fails.

النقص
السفه
الجزاء
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Afterlife pleasure is merely the removal of pain.
Correction: Al-Taftazani denies this, asserting that afterlife pleasure is a positive experience, not just relief from pain.

Checkpoint
According to the third objection, if resurrection is for the servant's benefit, what two possibilities are considered?
A) Reward and punishment
B) Pleasure and pain
C) Sensible and intellectual
D) Good and evil

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because it falsely assumes purpose is necessary and limits purposes to flawed options, whereas al-Taftazani shows that retribution is a valid purpose and afterlife pleasure is not merely pain relief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the ultimate retribution—justice from Allah—is a sufficient purpose for resurrection, what does that mean for how you view your own shortcomings? Are you truly living as if every deed will be repaid, or do you secretly hope that Allah will overlook your sins without consequence?
?
The objector claimed that causing pain for subsequent relief is not wise. Yet Allah allows pain in this world for eternal reward. Does this realization awaken in you a deeper trust in His wisdom, even when your present suffering seems pointless? Will you commit to surrendering your limited understanding to His perfect plan, starting today?

Dependence of Bodily Resurrection on Recreating the Non-Existent
Al-Taftazani states the rational impossibility of bodily resurrection due to dependence on recreating the non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why bodily resurrection, if it requires recreating what has perished, is considered rationally impossible according to the kalam argument.

Core Idea
Bodily resurrection requires recreating the non-existent, which is impossible.

Al-Taftazani concludes his discussion by stating clearly why bodily resurrection poses a rational problem. The key is the concept of recreating something that has completely ceased to exist. He then explains two ways this could be thought, but both lead to the same conclusion of impossibility. Let us follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the author's phrasing.
Notice the use of 'إما ... وأما' which structures the two alternatives.
2
Identify the core premise
The first sentence states the premise: bodily resurrection depends on recreating the non-existent. Ask yourself why this is problematic.
3
Examine the two options
The author gives two ways to conceive resurrection: creation after annihilation or gathering after dispersal. Reflect on why both fail according to the text.
The failure of the second option is due to the perishing of composition, mixture, and life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن المعاد الجسماني موقوف على إعادة المعدوم وقد بان استحالتها وجـه التوقف إما على تقدير كونها إيجاداً بعد الفناء فظاهر . وأما على تقدير كونها جمعاً وإحياء بعد التفرق والموت فللقطع بفناء التأليف والمزاج والحياة، وكثير من الأغراض والهيئات.
Indeed, bodily resurrection depends on recreating what has perished, and its impossibility has become clear. The reason for the dependence is either on the assumption that it is creation after annihilation, which is obvious [in its impossibility], or on the assumption that it is gathering and reviving after dispersal and death, because it is certain that the composition, mixture, life, and many of the accidents (a'rāḍ) and forms (hay'āt) have perished.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion by restating the core difficulty: bodily resurrection requires recreating something that has ceased to exist (i'ādat al-ma'dūm), and this is impossible. The author then clarifies two ways this could be conceived. First, if resurrection is considered as a new creation after the original's annihilation, its impossibility is obvious. Second, if it is seen as gathering and reviving the dispersed parts after death, it is still impossible because the specific composition, mixture, life, and many accidents and forms have perished. This argument reinforces the first objection, emphasizing that regardless of how one conceptualizes the process, the obstacles remain insurmountable without divine intervention.

Key Terms
إعادة المعدوم
الإيجاد بعد الفناء
الجمع بعد التفرق
الإحياء بعد الموت
التأليف
المزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Gathering after dispersal avoids the problem of recreating from nothing.
Correction: The text points out that the original composition has perished, so even gathering requires recreating the non-existent.

Checkpoint
According to the concluding passage, what are the two possible ways of conceiving bodily resurrection?
A) As a new creation or as a renovation
B) As creation after annihilation or as gathering after dispersal
C) As a spiritual return or as a physical return
D) As a miracle or as a natural process

Key Takeaway
Bodily resurrection is rationally problematic because both creation after annihilation and gathering after dispersal require recreating what has ceased to exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Your body ages and changes every day. What makes you so certain that the exact same body—with its memories, scars, and identity—will be raised on the Last Day? What evidence do you cling to that overrides this logical objection?

The Third Objection: Purposelessness of Resurrection and Its Rebuttal
Argues that restoration without a purpose is futile, and that possible purposes are problematic; the rebuttal denies these claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against bodily resurrection—that it is purposeless—and how the rebuttal refutes it by denying the necessity of purpose, expanding possible purposes, and affirming positive afterlife pleasure.

Core Idea
Resurrection needs no purpose; if it did, recompense is a valid one.

This passage presents the third objection to bodily resurrection: that it appears purposeless and any possible purpose leads to absurdity. The objection argues that if restoration has no purpose, it is futile; if it has a purpose for God, that implies deficiency; if for the servant, it must be either painful (foolish) or pleasurable—but pleasure in this world is merely relief from pain, so inflicting pain for future relief is unwise. The rebuttal counters by denying that a purpose is necessary, denying that purposes are limited to those mentioned (delivering recompense is valid), and denying that afterlife pleasure is merely relief from pain; it is a positive pleasure. Through this, the rationality of bodily resurrection is defended.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's structure
The objection lists three problematic possibilities: no purpose, purpose for God, purpose for servant. Understand why each is deemed unacceptable.
The objection assumes purpose must be either for God or for the servant.
2
Explore the specific argument about pleasure
Notice that the objection claims pleasure is merely relief from pain, so inflicting pain for future relief is not wise. This is a key point.
The objection views pleasure as purely negative.
3
Learn the three rebuttal denials
The rebuttal denies: (1) that purpose is necessary, (2) that purposes are limited to those listed (recompense is another), (3) that afterlife pleasure is removal of pain.
The rebuttal offers a positive alternative.
4
Connect to the broader theology
Understand that this defense upholds the rationality of bodily resurrection, which is essential for Islamic eschatology.
This demonstrates that reason and revelation align.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - أن الإعادة لا لغرض عبث ، ولغرض عائد إلى الله تعالى نقص، وإلى العبد إما إيصال ألم، وهو سفه، أو لذة، ولا لذة في الوجود، سيما في عالم الحس إذ هي خلاص عن ألم والإيلام ليعقبه الخلاص غير لائق بالحكمة . بمنع لزوم الغرض، ومنع انحصاره فيما ذكر، إذ ربما يكون إيصال الجزاء إلى المستحق غرضاً، ومنع كون اللذة الأخروية دفع الألم).
Third - That restoration without a purpose is futile, and with a purpose that returns to God is a deficiency, and to the servant either the infliction of pain, which is foolish, or pleasure, and there is no pleasure in existence, especially in the sensory world, since it is relief from pain, and inflicting pain to be followed by relief is not befitting wisdom. It is rebutted by denying the necessity of a purpose, and denying its limitation to what was mentioned, for perhaps delivering recompense to its due is a purpose, and denying that the pleasure of the afterlife is the removal of pain.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection against bodily resurrection and its rebuttal. The objection argues that restoration must have a purpose, otherwise it is futile. If the purpose pertains to God, it implies deficiency; if to the servant, it is either inflicting pain (which is foolish) or pleasure. But there is no true pleasure in existence, especially in the sensory world, because pleasure is merely the removal of pain. Inflicting pain for later relief is not befitting wisdom. The rebuttal denies three points: first, it is not necessary that restoration have a purpose; second, even if it does, the purpose is not limited to what the objection mentions—for example, delivering recompense to the deserving is a valid purpose; third, the assumption that the pleasure of the afterlife is relief from pain is denied (i.e., it is a real, positive pleasure). Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
الغرض (al-gharad)
العبث (al-'abath)
اللذة (al-ladhdha)
الألم (al-alam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pleasure is only the removal of pain, so there is no positive pleasure.
Correction: The rebuttal denies that afterlife pleasure is merely relief from pain; it is a real, positive pleasure.

Checkpoint

A) To demonstrate God's power.
B) Delivering recompense to the deserving.
C) To create new beings.
D) To test humans again.

Key Takeaway
The third objection fails because resurrection does not require a purpose, and even if it did, delivering just recompense is a valid purpose, and afterlife pleasure is genuinely positive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The objection claims that inflicting pain for later pleasure is unwise. Have you ever experienced a period of intense difficulty that later brought you closer to God or taught you something valuable? Does that change how you view divine wisdom in your current hardships?
?
If resurrection does not need a purpose, yet God still chooses to recompense you, does your trust in His justice increase? Are you living today in a way that reflects confidence in that ultimate recompense, or are you seeking immediate relief from pain?

The First Objection: Returning the Non-Existent
First objection against bodily resurrection and its rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against bodily resurrection—that it requires returning the non-existent (i'adat al-ma'dum)—and how classical theologians rebutted it by denying the premises and redefining resurrection as restoration of parts.

Core Idea
Resurrection does not require bringing back nothing; it requires restoring the body's parts.

One of the most common philosophical objections to bodily resurrection is that it would require God to bring back something that has completely ceased to exist—an impossibility. In this passage, the author presents this objection and then shows how the theologian rebuts it by denying the impossibility and clarifying what resurrection actually entails. Let's examine this foundational debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is stated. Note the key claim: resurrection is based on 'return of the non-existent' (i'adat al-ma'dum) and that the body's components are annihilated.
The objection relies on two premises.
2
Understand the two premises being denied
The rebuttal denies both premises: (1) that the body is completely annihilated, and (2) that returning the non-existent is impossible. See how the author says 'rebutted by denying the two premises.'
This is a standard kalam technique.
3
Grasp the redefinition of resurrection
The author concedes for argument's sake but clarifies that resurrection means restoring parts to their previous composition and life, not returning the exact same entity. So the resurrected body is 'like' the beginning (mabda'), not identical.
This subtle shift avoids the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الأول) - انه مبني على إعادة المعدوم للقطع بفناء المزاج والحياة والتأليف والهيئات وقد ثبت استحالتها ورد بمنع المقدمتين . والجواب - منع امتناع الإعادة. وقد تكلمنا على أدلته ، ولوسلم، فالمراد إعادة الأجزاء إلى ما كانت عليه من التأليف والحياة، ونحو ذلك . ولا يضرنا كون المعاد مثل المبدأ، لاعينه.
(First) - It is based on the return of the non-existent, due to the certainty of the annihilation of temperament, life, composition, and states, and their impossibility has been established; it is rebutted by denying the two premises. The answer: denial of the impossibility of restoration. And we have already spoken about its proofs, and if it is conceded, then the meaning is the restoration of the parts to what they were of composition, life, and so forth. And it does not harm us that the restored (ma'ad) is like the beginning (mabda'), not the same as it.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against bodily resurrection and its rebuttal. The objection argues that bodily resurrection depends on the return of the non-existent (i'adat al-ma'dum), because temperament, life, composition, and states are known to perish. Since returning what has ceased to exist is impossible, resurrection is impossible. The rebuttal is simply to deny the two premises: the premise that the body is entirely annihilated and the premise that returning the non-existent is impossible. The book states that the impossibility of restoration is denied, and previous arguments have already addressed its proofs. Even if one concedes the impossibility, the intended meaning of resurrection is the restoration of the parts to their previous composition and life, not the return of the exact same entity in every respect. Therefore, it does not matter if the resurrected body is like the original rather than identical in all aspects.

Key Terms
إعادة المعدوم (i'adat al-ma'dum)
المعاد (al-ma'ad)
المبدأ (al-mabda')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the rebuttal accepts the impossibility of returning the non-existent.
Correction: The rebuttal denies the impossibility or, if conceded, redefines resurrection as restoration of parts, not return of the exact non-existent.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the first objection against bodily resurrection?
A) Resurrection requires the return of the non-existent, which is impossible.
B) The body is not annihilated so no return is needed.
C) The soul is immortal and does not need a body.
D) The Qur'an does not mention bodily resurrection.

Key Takeaway
The first objection to bodily resurrection is based on the impossibility of returning the non-existent, but theologians rebut it by denying the impossibility of restoration and clarifying that resurrection means restoring the body's parts to their previous state, not returning the exact same entity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you living as if your current identity is tied to this specific material body, or are you preparing for a body that will be like it but better? What practical step can you take today to orient your heart toward that reality?
?
This passage defends resurrection through rational argument. But do you treat the resurrection as a mere intellectual doctrine to be defended, or as a truth that commands your entire life? If you truly believed that every atom of your body will be restored and judged, what would you change about your daily habits starting tomorrow?

The Second Objection: Cannibalism and Original Parts
Al-Sanusi presents the cannibalism objection and solves it via original parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the cannibalism objection to bodily resurrection and the Ash'ari solution based on original parts (al-ajza' al-asliyya).

Core Idea
Only the original parts are resurrected, not those acquired from food.

The cannibalism objection challenges bodily resurrection by asking which body gets the shared parts. This objection was historically used by skeptics and materialists. Al-Sanusi defends the doctrine by introducing the concept of 'original parts' (al-ajza' al-asliyya) that persist throughout life. In this passage, you will see the objection, the rebuttal, and a deeper discussion with scriptural evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the argument's structure emerge naturally.
The passage is dense with objections and answers; note the repetitive structure.
2
Identify the core problem
What is the cannibalism objection? How does it threaten the idea of bodily resurrection? Look for the claim that parts cannot belong to two bodies.
The objection raises issues of identity and justice.
3
Understand the solution: original parts
Al-Sanusi introduces 'al-ajza' al-asliyya' (original parts). What are they? How do they solve the problem? Notice the contrast with parts acquired through nutrition (al-taghdhiya).
Original parts are those present from birth until death.
4
Examine the scriptural response
The passage includes a quotation about reviving bones and dust. How does Al-Sanusi interpret these verses? He distinguishes between verses that remove astonishment and those that prove restoration.
This shows the importance of proper scriptural hermeneutics.
5
Compare with the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila assert that God must preserve parts for justice. Al-Sanusi says 'perhaps' (la'alla). What is the difference in theological method?
Ash'aris emphasize divine will and power; Mu'tazila emphasize divine wisdom and obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - لو أكل إنسان إنساناً فالأجزاء المأكولة إما أن تعاد في بدن الأكل، فلا يكون المأكول بعينه معاداً أو بالعكس، على أن لا أولوية، ولا سبيل إلى جعلها جزءاً من كل منهما، وأنه يلزم في أكل الكافر المؤمن تنعيم الأجزاء العاصية، وتعذيب المطيعة، ورد بأن المعاد هي الأجزاء الأصلية، فلا محذور، ولعل الله يحفظها من أن تصير جزءاً أصلياً لبدن آخر . بل عند المعتزلة يجب ذلك ليصل الجزاء إلى مستحقه . فإن قيل: مثل من يحيي العظام وهي رميم أئذا متنا وكنا تراباً يشعر بأن المتنازع إعادة الأجزاء بأسرها. قلنا لأنه ورد إزالة لاستبعادهم إحياء الرميم والتراب، والوارد لإثبات نفس الإعادة ايضاً كثير مثل : وهو الذي يبدأ الخلق ثم يعيده فسيقولون من يعيدنا قل الذي فطركم أول مرة . الثاني - لو أكل إنسان إنساناً، وصار غذاء له جزءاً من بدنه، فالأجزاء المأكولة إما أن تعاد في بدن الآكل، أو في بدن المأكول، وأياً ما كان لا يكون أحدهما بعينه معاداً بتمامه على أنه لا أولوية لجعلها جزءاً من بدن أحدهما دون الآخرة ولا سبيل لجعلها جزءاً من كل منهما، وايضاً إذا كان الأكل كافراً والمأكول مؤمناً يلزم تنعيم الأجزاء العاصية ، أو تعذيب الأجزاء المطيعة. والجواب - أنا نعني بالحشر إعادة الأجزاء الأصلية الباقية من أول العمر إلى آخره، لا الحاصلة بالتغذية ، فالمعاد من كل من الأكل والمأكول الأجزاء الأصلية الحاصلة في أول الفطرة من غير لزوم فساد. فإن قيل : يجوز أن يصير تلك الأجزاء الغذائية الأصلية في المأكول الفاضل في الأكل نطفة وأجزاء أصلية لبدن آخر، ويعود المحذور. قلنا : الفساد إنما هو في وقوع ذلك ، لا في إمكانه فلعل الله تعالى يحفظها من أن تصير جزءاً لبدن آخر، فضلاً عن أن تصير جزءاً أصلياً. وقد ادعى المعتزلة أنه يجب على الحكيم حفظها عن ذلك ليتمكن من إيصال الجزاء إلى مستحقه، ونحن نقول : لعله يحفظها عن التفرق، فلا يحتاج إلى إعادة الجمع والتأليف، بل إنما يعاد إلى الحياة والصور والهيئات.
Second - If a person eats another person, the eaten parts are either restored in the body of the eater, then the eaten person is not restored as the same individual, or vice versa, without priority, and there is no way to make them a part of both bodies, and it necessitates in the case of a disbeliever eating a believer the enjoyment of the disobedient parts and the punishment of the obedient parts. It is rebutted by saying that the restored parts are the original parts (al-ajza' al-asliyya), so there is no problem, and perhaps God preserves them from becoming an original part of another body. Rather, according to the Mu'tazila, it is necessary for that to occur so that recompense reaches its due. If it is said: Like 'Who revives the bones when they are decayed?' 'When we are dead and become dust?' suggests that the dispute is about the restoration of all parts. We say: It was revealed to remove their astonishment at reviving decayed bones and dust, and what is revealed to prove restoration itself is also abundant, such as: 'He is the One who originates creation and then restores it' and 'They will say: Who will restore us? Say: The One who created you the first time.' Second - If a person eats another person, and the food becomes part of his body, the eaten parts are either restored in the body of the eater or in the body of the eaten, and in either case, neither one is restored exactly in its entirety, since there is no priority to make them part of one body over the other, and there is no way to make them part of both bodies, and also if the eater is a disbeliever and the eaten is a believer, it necessitates the enjoyment of the disobedient parts or the punishment of the obedient parts. The answer: We mean by resurrection (al-hashr) the restoration of the original parts that remain from the beginning of life to its end, not those obtained through nutrition. So the restored (al-ma'ad) from each of the eater and the eaten are the original parts obtained at the first creation, without any corruption. If it is said: It is possible that those nutritional parts become the surplus in the eaten, a sperm and original parts for another body, and the problem returns. We say: Corruption is only in the occurrence of that, not in its possibility; perhaps God preserves them from becoming a part of another body, let alone becoming an original part. The Mu'tazila claimed that it is incumbent on the Wise to preserve them from that to enable the recompense to reach its due. We say: Perhaps He preserves them from dispersion, so there is no need to restore gathering and composition, but rather they are restored to life, forms, and states.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage addresses the second objection to bodily resurrection: the problem of cannibalism. If one human eats another, the consumed parts could belong to both individuals at resurrection. The objection presents several difficulties: if the parts are restored to the eater's body, the eaten person is not fully restored; if to the eaten, the eater is incomplete; there is no priority to assign them to one body; and if a believer is eaten by a disbeliever, the believer's parts would be rewarded (since they become part of the disbeliever's body) and the disbeliever's parts punished, which is unjust. The initial rebuttal states that only the original parts (al-ajza' al-asliyya) are resurrected, not those acquired through nutrition. God may preserve those original parts from becoming original parts of another body. A further objection quotes verses that mention reviving bones and dust, suggesting that all parts are restored. The answer clarifies that these verses are meant to remove astonishment, not to prove the restoration of every particle; other verses explicitly prove restoration. The passage then repeats the objection in more detail and gives the answer: resurrection restores the original parts that persist from birth to death, not the nutritional parts. A sub-objection asks if the nutritional parts could become original parts for another body (e.g., as sperm), and the answer is that corruption lies in the actual occurrence, not the possibility, and God may preserve them. The Mu'tazila assert that God must preserve them to ensure justice, while the author says perhaps He preserves them from dispersion, so there is no need to recompose scattered parts.

Key Terms
الأجزاء الأصلية
الحشر
المعاد
التغذية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that bodily resurrection requires the exact same material particles that composed the body at death.
Correction: Al-Sanusi clarifies that only the original parts (al-ajza' al-asliyya) are restored, not the parts acquired through nutrition or other changes.

Checkpoint
How does the author resolve the cannibalism objection?
A) By affirming that all eaten parts are restored in the eater's body.
B) By stating that only the original parts (al-ajza' al-asliyya) are resurrected.
C) By denying that cannibalism ever occurs.
D) By claiming that the soul alone is resurrected.

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of original parts (al-ajza' al-asliyya) resolves the cannibalism objection and affirms bodily resurrection while maintaining divine justice and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What parts of your life—habits, attachments, relationships—are you counting on to be eternally 'you'? Have you considered that only your original self (your core intentionality and faith) will be restored, not your acquired accumulations? How does that change what you prioritize daily?
?
If God preserves the original parts of every person from dispersion, as Al-Sanusi argues, then your resurrection is certain. Yet do you live as if you will face every intention and action with that same core self? What have you added to your life that you assume will be resurrected as part of you, but is actually temporary?
?
The Mu'tazila demanded that God must preserve parts for justice; Al-Sanusi said 'perhaps He preserves them.' Are you demanding that God owes you a certain outcome in the afterlife, or are you trusting His will and power? Which of your expectations about the Day of Judgment is based on a sense of entitlement rather than reliance on God's mercy and wisdom?

The Objection from Cannibalism and Its Answer
Presents the second objection about cannibalism and the response distinguishing original parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that cannibalism poses for bodily resurrection and how the response distinguishes original parts from acquired parts.

Core Idea
Resurrection restores only the original parts, not those gained through nutrition.

This lesson tackles a classic objection against bodily resurrection: what happens if one person eats another? If the consumed parts become part of the eater's body, how can both be resurrected completely? The answer clarifies that resurrection concerns only the original parts that persist from birth, avoiding the dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the objection presents a logical dilemma and a moral dilemma.
2
Understand the dilemma of parts
The eaten parts cannot be returned to both the eater and the eaten simultaneously. There is no priority for one over the other, and they cannot be split. Why is this a problem for resurrection?
3
Consider the added moral problem
If the eater is a disbeliever and the eaten a believer, then the same parts would have to be rewarded (for being part of the believer) and punished (for being part of the disbeliever). This seems contradictory.
This highlights the incoherence of a naive view of bodily resurrection.
4
Study the answer
The answer defines resurrection (al-ḥashr) as restoring only the original parts that persist from birth to death, not those gained through nutrition. This resolves both dilemmas.
Observe how the definition of terms is key to resolving theological objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - لو أكل إنسان إنساناً، وصار غذاء له جزءاً من بدنه، فالأجزاء المأكولة إما أن تعاد في بدن الآكل، أو في بدن المأكول، وأياً ما كان لا يكون أحدهما بعينه معاداً بتمامه على أنه لا أولوية لجعلها جزءاً من بدن أحدهما دون الآخرة ولا سبيل لجعلها جزءاً من كل منهما، وايضاً إذا كان الأكل كافراً والمأكول مؤمناً يلزم تنعيم الأجزاء العاصية ، أو تعذيب الأجزاء المطيعة. والجواب - أنا نعني بالحشر إعادة الأجزاء الأصلية الباقية من أول العمر إلى آخره، لا الحاصلة بالتغذية ، فالمعاد من كل من الأكل والمأكول الأجزاء الأصلية الحاصلة في أول الفطرة من غير لزوم فساد.
Second: If a human eats a human, and it becomes nutrition for him a part of his body, then the eaten parts either are returned in the body of the eater or in the body of the eaten. Whichever it is, neither of the two is entirely returned as the very same, given that there is no priority for making them a part of the body of one over the other, and no way to make them a part of each of the two. Also, if the eater is a disbeliever and the eaten a believer, it would necessitate rewarding the disobedient parts or punishing the obedient parts. The answer: We mean by the gathering (al-ḥashr) the restoration of the original parts that remain from the beginning of life to its end, not those acquired through nutrition. So the resurrected of each of the eater and the eaten is the original parts that came into being at the first creation, without any corruption necessarily following.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against bodily resurrection and its answer. The objection rests on the impossibility of reassigning consumed body parts: they cannot be restored fully to both the eater and the eaten. Furthermore, if the eater is a disbeliever and the eaten a believer, rewarding or punishing the parts becomes incoherent. The answer clarifies that resurrection ('al-ḥashr') means restoring only the original parts that persist from birth to death, not those gained through nutrition. Thus each person is resurrected from their own original parts, avoiding the dilemma.

Key Terms

الأجزاء الأصلية
الحشر
التغذية
المعاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think resurrection requires reassembling the exact same atoms that composed the body at death, including atoms gained from food.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only the original parts that remain from birth to death are restored; acquired parts from nutrition are not necessary for resurrection.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what does 'al-ḥashr' refer to?
A) The restoration of all body parts including those acquired through nutrition.
B) The restoration of only the original parts that persist from birth to death.
C) The restoration of the soul only, not the body.
D) The resurrection of the entire body exactly as it was at death.

Key Takeaway
Resurrection restores only the original parts that accompany a person from birth to death, not those gained through nutrition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If your identity after resurrection depends on the original parts from birth to death, what does that say about the value of your physical actions? How does this affect your view of your body as a trust from God?
?
This answer shows that no human action can thwart God's plan of resurrection. Does your heart truly accept that God's power is absolute over every detail of your life and death? What practical steps can you take to deepen that conviction?

Could Original Parts Become Another's?
Raises an objection and provides an answer about original parts becoming another's.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that original parts could become another's original parts is answered by distinguishing possibility from actuality and appealing to divine preservation.

Core Idea
Possibility does not entail actuality; God can preserve original parts intact.

This passage addresses a further objection to the doctrine of bodily resurrection with original parts. The opponent argues that even if we accept resurrection of original parts, those parts could become the original parts of another body through digestion and reproduction, bringing back the original difficulty. The answer distinguishes between mere possibility and actual occurrence, and asserts that God can preserve those parts from such transformation. The Mu'tazila argue it is obligatory for a Wise God to do so, but the author takes a more modest position: perhaps God preserves them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the objection and the answer. Notice the structure: it begins with "If it is said..." and then "We say..."
2
Identify the two parts
The passage has an objection and an answer. The objection is about original food parts becoming another's original parts. The answer first distinguishes possibility from actuality, then mentions the Mu'tazila claim and the author's position.
The answer uses 'perhaps' (لعل) which shows a cautious tone.
3
Understand the Mu'tazilite argument
The Mu'tazila claim it is obligatory for the Wise (God) to preserve the parts so that retribution can be delivered. The author agrees that preservation is possible but uses 'perhaps' rather than 'obligatory'.
Compare the different theological orientations: Mu'tazila emphasize divine justice, while the author emphasizes divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : يجوز أن يصير تلك الأجزاء الغذائية الأصلية في المأكول الفضل في الأكل نطفة وأجزاء أصلية لبدن ،آخر، ويعود المحذور. قلنا : الفساد إنما هو في وقوع ذلك ، لا في إمكانه فلعل الله تعالى يحفظها من أن تصير جزءاً لبدن ،آخر، فضلاً عن أن تصير جزءاً أصلياً. وقد ادعى المعتزلة (1) أنه يجب على الحكيم حفظها عن ذلك ليتمكن من إيصال الجزاء إلى مستحقه، ونحن نقول : لعله يحفظها عن التفرق، فلا يحتاج إلى إعادة الجمع والتأليف، بل إنما يعاد إلى الحياة والصور والهيئات.
If it is said: It is possible that those original food parts in the eaten become (by way of the eater's surplus) sperm and original parts for another body, and the problem returns. We say: The corruption is only in its actual occurrence, not in its possibility. Perhaps God Almighty protects them from becoming a part of another body, let alone from becoming an original part. The Mu'tazila claimed that it is obligatory for the Wise to protect them from that in order to be able to deliver the recompense to its deserving one. We say: Perhaps He protects them from scattering, so there is no need for re-gathering and re-composing; rather, they are only restored to life and forms and shapes.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents a further objection: even if resurrection is of original parts, those original food parts could become the sperm (original parts) of another body, bringing back the same difficulty. The answer distinguishes between possibility and actuality: the corruption lies in its actual occurrence, not its mere possibility. God could preserve those parts from ever becoming part of another body, especially from becoming original parts. The Mu'tazila argued that it is obligatory for a Wise God to preserve them to ensure retribution, but the author says God might even preserve them from disintegrating, such that they do not need to be reassembled but only restored to life and form.

Key Terms
الأجزاء الأصلية
المعتزلة
الحكيم
الحفظ
التفرق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the problem is logically inescapable; the answer shows it is only a possibility that God can prevent.
Correction: The objection confuses possibility with actuality. God's power to preserve ensures the actual occurrence can be avoided, so the doctrine remains coherent.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazilite claim mentioned in the passage?
A) That God must preserve the parts because it is impossible for Him to do otherwise.
B) That it is obligatory for the Wise to preserve the parts so that retribution can be delivered.
C) That resurrection is purely spiritual and does not involve bodies.
D) That the parts will inevitably become parts of another body.

Key Takeaway
The objection to resurrection with original parts is not fatal because God can preserve them from ever becoming another's parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazila insisted that God must act a certain way to be wise and just. Have you ever imposed your own expectations of what God 'should' do? Are you willing to trust His 'perhaps' even when you cannot see the outcome, especially regarding your own final state?
?
This lesson shows that God can prevent any obstacle to resurrection. What obstacles in your own life—sins, doubts, or attachments—do you need to trust God to remove, even if you cannot imagine how? Will you commit today to surrender that specific struggle to Him and wait on His preservation?

Responding to the Objection from Resurrection Verses
Objection from resurrection verses, response distinguishing re-creation from identity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that Quranic verses require resurrection of all body parts is answered by distinguishing between the deniers' concern and the theological subtlety of identity.

Core Idea
The verses prove God's power to re-create, not necessarily every atom.

In theological debates about bodily resurrection, opponents sometimes cite Quranic verses that mention decayed bones or dust being revived. At first glance, these seem to demand exact material continuity. However, the response shows that the intended lesson of these verses is different: they aim to remove doubt about God's power to bring back the dead, not to settle philosophical questions about the identity of particles. This passage clarifies that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the verses cited
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the verses the objector uses: Q 36:78, 56:47, 34:7, which speak of decayed bones, dust, and being torn apart.
2
Understand the response's move
The response cites other verses (Q 30:27, 17:51) that emphasize God's ability to begin and repeat creation. The key is that the deniers' doubt was about the possibility of revival, not about which particles are restored.
The response does not argue against the objector's verses; it recontextualizes them.
3
Reflect on the theological nuance
Realize that the question of restoring original parts versus all parts was not the focus of the deniers. The response shows that the Quran's aim is to affirm God's power, not to settle philosophical debates about identity.
This keeps the discussion on the intended meaning of the verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الآيات الواردة في باب الحشر من مثل : من يحيي العظام وهي رميم (۲) أئذا متنا وكنا ترابا (۳) إذا مزقتم كل ممزق إنكم لفي خلق جديد (4) . تشعر بأن الأصلية، وغير الأصلية، ومتنازع المحق والمبطل، ومتوارد الإثبات والنفي هي إعادة الأجزاء بأسرها إلى الحياة ، لا الأصلية وحدها، ولا إعادة المعدوم بعينه . قلنا: ومن الآيات ما هو مسوق لنفس الإعادة مثل : وهو الذي يبدأ الخلق ثم يعيده) (۱) فسيقولون من يعيدنا قل الذي فطركم أول مرة ) (٢) وكأن المنكرين استبعدوا إحياء ما كانوا يشاهدون من الرميم والتراب، فأزيل استبعادهم بتذكير ابتداء الفطرة والتنبيه على كمال العلم والقدرة. وأما حديث إعادة المعدوم والأجزاء الأصلية فلعله لم يخطر ببالهم .
If it is said: The verses that have come in the chapter of al-ḥashr, such as 'Who will give life to bones while they are decayed?' (Q 36:78), 'When we die and become dust?' (Q 56:47), 'When you are torn apart every tearing, you will be in a new creation' (Q 34:7) — suggest that [what is meant is] the original and non-original, that which is disputed between the right and the wrong, and that which is subject to affirmation and negation, is the restoration of all parts to life, not the original ones alone, nor the restoration of the non-existent as the very same. We say: Among the verses are those that are presented for the [act of] restoration itself, such as 'He who begins creation then repeats it' (Q 30:27), 'They will say: Who will give us life? Say: He who created you the first time' (Q 17:51). It is as if the deniers considered the revival of what they witnessed of decayed bones and dust far-fetched, so their far-fetchedness was removed by reminding them of the first creation and drawing attention to the perfection of knowledge and power. As for the talk of restoring the non-existent and the original parts, it perhaps never occurred to them.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage raises an objection from Quranic verses that seem to indicate the resurrection of all body parts, including non-original ones, and not merely the original parts. The answer points to other verses that focus on the act of re-creation itself, such as 'He who begins creation then repeats it' and 'Say: He who created you the first time.' The real issue for the deniers was the revival of decayed bones and dust, which God's power makes possible. The subtle question of restoring exactly the same or only original parts was not on their minds. Thus these verses do not force the conclusion that every atom must be restored.

Key Terms
الآيات
الحشر
الإعادة
المعدوم
الأجزاء الأصلية
الرميم
التراب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran requires exact material continuity of all parts for resurrection.
Correction: The verses focus on God's power to create anew; the question of exact particle identity was not the concern of the deniers.

Checkpoint
What is the essence of the answer to the objection from Quranic verses?
A) The verses prove that all parts must be restored exactly as they were.
B) The verses are metaphorical and do not concern bodily resurrection.
C) The deniers' concern was the revival of decayed bones, not the identity of particles, and other verses emphasize re-creation.
D) The verses only refer to original parts.

Key Takeaway
Quranic verses about resurrection primarily affirm God's power to re-create, not necessarily the restoration of every specific atom.

The Objection: Resurrection Has No Proper Purpose
Objection enumerating three invalid purposes for resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three-part objection that bodily resurrection appears purposeless and why each proposed purpose is deemed invalid.

Core Idea
The objection claims resurrection is either futile or serves an unworthy purpose.

In this passage, the objector argues that bodily resurrection cannot have a meaningful purpose. They consider three possibilities—futility, a purpose for God, or a purpose for creatures—and find each unacceptable for a wise agent. This objection challenges the very rationale for resurrection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall argument. Then read the translation to confirm understanding.
Notice the logical structure: the objector enumerates three possibilities and rejects each.
2
Identify the three categories of purpose
The objector considers: 1) futility, 2) a purpose returning to God, 3) a purpose returning to creatures. Distinguish each and see why each is rejected.
The rejection of a purpose for creatures is further subdivided into pain and pleasure.
3
Reflect on the philosophical basis
Think about why the objector assumes a wise agent must act for a purpose and why that purpose cannot be futile or self-serving. This sets up the need for a refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - أن الإعادة لا لغرض عبث لا يليق بالحكيم ، ولغرض عائد إلى الله تعالى نقص يجب تنزيهه عنه، ولغرض عائد إلى العباد ايضاً باطل، لأنه إما إيصال ألم ، وهو لا يليق بالحكيم، وإما إيصال ،لذة ولا لذة في الوجود، سيما في عالم الحس. فكل ما يتخيل لذة فإنما هو خلاص عن الألم، ولا ألم في العدم أو الموت، ليكون الخلاص عنه لذة مقصودة بالإعادة ، بل إنما يتصور ذلك بأن يوصل إليه ألماً ثم يخلصه عنه، فتكون الاعادة لإيصال ألم يعقبه خلاص (۳) وهو غير لائق بالحكمة (٤) . والجواب - منع الزوم الغرض، وقبح الخلو عنه في فعل الله تعالى. ثم منع انحصار الغرض في إيصال اللذة والألم () ، إذ يجوز أن يكون نفس إيصال الجزاء إلى من يستحقه ،غرضاً ، ثم منع كون اللذة دفعاً للألم، وخلاصاً عنه . كيف واللذة والألم من الوجدانيات التي لا يشك العاقل في تحققها، وقد سبق تحقيق
Third — That restoration has no purpose: futility is unbefitting of the Wise; a purpose returning to God is a deficiency from which He must be exalted; and a purpose returning to the servants is also invalid, because it is either the delivery of pain, which is unbefitting of the Wise, or the delivery of pleasure, and there is no pleasure in existence, especially in the sensory world. For everything imagined as pleasure is but deliverance from pain, and there is no pain in non-existence or death such that deliverance from it would be a pleasure intended by restoration. Rather, that can only be imagined by delivering pain to one and then delivering him from it, so restoration would be for delivering pain followed by deliverance — and that is not befitting of wisdom. The answer: Denial of the necessity of purpose and the ugliness of being devoid of it in God's action. Then denial that purpose is restricted to delivering pleasure and pain, for it is possible that the very delivery of recompense to its deserving one is a purpose. Then denial that pleasure is repulsion of pain and deliverance from it. How so, when pleasure and pain are among the direct experiences (al-wijdāniyyāt) whose reality no rational person doubts, and this has been established.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection against bodily resurrection: it must have a purpose, but it cannot be futile, nor for God's benefit (since He transcends need), nor for the benefit of creatures (since that would involve either pain or pleasure, and pleasure is merely removal of pain, but there is no pain in death). Thus resurrection would cause unnecessary pain, which is unbefitting of a wise agent. The answer refutes each point: first, God is not subject to needing a purpose; second, the purpose is precisely to deliver just recompense; third, pleasure is not merely the removal of pain—pleasure and pain are direct experiences (al-wijdāniyyāt) whose reality is self-evident, as has been established elsewhere.

Key Terms
الغرض
العبث
الحكيم
اللذة
الألم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why is a purpose returning to creatures invalid?
A) Because it would involve delivering pain, which is unbefitting of the Wise.
B) Because it would involve delivering pleasure, and pleasure does not exist in the sensory world.
C) Both A and B are reasons.
D) Because creatures do not deserve any recompense.

Key Takeaway
The objection asserts that resurrection cannot have a purpose consistent with divine wisdom, as all possible purposes are shown to be invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector claims that all pleasure is merely relief from pain. Think of the last time you felt true pleasure—was it really just an escape from discomfort? How does this observation unsettle your understanding of why you seek pleasure in this life?
?

?
The objection argues that delivering pain as a purpose is unbefitting of the Wise. Yet you know that God has created a world with immense suffering. How do you reconcile this with the objection's claim? Is the objection valid, or is there a purpose beyond your grasp?

The Refutation: Three Denials
Refutation of the objection through three denials.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three-pronged refutation that denies the necessity of purpose, the restriction of purpose to pain/pleasure, and the reducibility of pleasure to pain removal.

Core Idea
The answer shows that God is not bound by purpose, recompense itself is a purpose, and pleasure is an irreducible experience.

In this passage, the author directly counters the objection by denying its key assumptions. He first denies that God must act for a purpose at all. Then he denies that purpose is limited to delivering pain or pleasure. Finally, he denies that pleasure is merely the removal of pain, insisting that pleasure and pain are direct, self-evident experiences.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice the three instances of 'منع' (denial).
The answer is concise but powerful.
2
Identify the three denials
List the three denials: 1) Denial of the necessity of purpose in God's actions. 2) Denial that purpose is restricted to pleasure/pain. 3) Denial that pleasure is merely relief from pain.
Each denial targets a specific claim in the objection.
3
Understand the justification for each denial
For the first, the author simply asserts that purpose is not necessary. For the second, he states that delivering just recompense is a valid purpose. For the third, he appeals to the self-evident nature of pleasure and pain as direct experiences (al-wijdāniyyāt).
The third denial is supported by a reference to prior proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - أن الإعادة لا لغرض عبث لا يليق بالحكيم ، ولغرض عائد إلى الله تعالى نقص يجب تنزيهه عنه، ولغرض عائد إلى العباد ايضاً باطل، لأنه إما إيصال ألم ، وهو لا يليق بالحكيم، وإما إيصال ،لذة ولا لذة في الوجود، سيما في عالم الحس. فكل ما يتخيل لذة فإنما هو خلاص عن الألم، ولا ألم في العدم أو الموت، ليكون الخلاص عنه لذة مقصودة بالإعادة ، بل إنما يتصور ذلك بأن يوصل إليه ألماً ثم يخلصه عنه، فتكون الاعادة لإيصال ألم يعقبه خلاص (۳) وهو غير لائق بالحكمة (٤) . والجواب - منع الزوم الغرض، وقبح الخلو عنه في فعل الله تعالى. ثم منع انحصار الغرض في إيصال اللذة والألم () ، إذ يجوز أن يكون نفس إيصال الجزاء إلى من يستحقه ،غرضاً ، ثم منع كون اللذة دفعاً للألم، وخلاصاً عنه . كيف واللذة والألم من الوجدانيات التي لا يشك العاقل في تحققها، وقد سبق تحقيق
Third — That restoration has no purpose: futility is unbefitting of the Wise; a purpose returning to God is a deficiency from which He must be exalted; and a purpose returning to the servants is also invalid, because it is either the delivery of pain, which is unbefitting of the Wise, or the delivery of pleasure, and there is no pleasure in existence, especially in the sensory world. For everything imagined as pleasure is but deliverance from pain, and there is no pain in non-existence or death such that deliverance from it would be a pleasure intended by restoration. Rather, that can only be imagined by delivering pain to one and then delivering him from it, so restoration would be for delivering pain followed by deliverance — and that is not befitting of wisdom. The answer: Denial of the necessity of purpose and the ugliness of being devoid of it in God's action. Then denial that purpose is restricted to delivering pleasure and pain, for it is possible that the very delivery of recompense to its deserving one is a purpose. Then denial that pleasure is repulsion of pain and deliverance from it. How so, when pleasure and pain are among the direct experiences (al-wijdāniyyāt) whose reality no rational person doubts, and this has been established.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection against bodily resurrection: it must have a purpose, but it cannot be futile, nor for God's benefit (since He transcends need), nor for the benefit of creatures (since that would involve either pain or pleasure, and pleasure is merely removal of pain, but there is no pain in death). Thus resurrection would cause unnecessary pain, which is unbefitting of a wise agent. The answer refutes each point: first, God is not subject to needing a purpose; second, the purpose is precisely to deliver just recompense; third, pleasure is not merely the removal of pain—pleasure and pain are direct experiences (al-wijdāniyyāt) whose reality is self-evident, as has been established elsewhere.

Key Terms
المنع
الغرض
اللذة
الألم
الوجدانيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pleasure is merely the removal of pain.
Correction: Pleasure and pain are direct experiences (al-wijdāniyyāt) whose reality is self-evident, not reducible to each other.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three denials in the answer?
A) Denial that purpose is necessary in God's actions.
B) Denial that purpose is restricted to pleasure and pain.
C) Denial that delivering recompense can be a purpose.
D) Denial that pleasure is merely the removal of pain.

Key Takeaway
The refutation defends resurrection by denying that God needs a purpose and by affirming that delivering just recompense is a valid purpose, while also asserting the irreducible nature of pleasure and pain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever questioned God's fairness because of pain you endured? How does the denial that God must have a purpose in the way we expect change the way you view your trials?
?
The passage says pleasure is irreducible—not just the absence of pain. Think of the deepest pleasure you have experienced. Did you treat it as a mere relief from something else, or did you allow it to be a pure gift from Allah? What would change if you saw every pleasure as a direct experience from Him?
?
Recompense to the deserving is described as a valid purpose in itself. How does this shape your intention when you do good deeds? Are you seeking only the reward or also aligning with the very purpose of justice that Allah has established?
